The Absence of Conjunction Between Basmalah and Hamdalah
The author explains the reason for omitting the conjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the twofold reason the poet omitted the conjunction between basmalah and hamdalah, including the grammatical rule.

Core Idea
Omission of conjunction is intentional and grammatically sound.

The author of the poem did not place a waaw (and) between the basmalah and the hamdalah. This lesson unpacks two reasons: each phrase suffices alone for the purpose of opening, and the two expressions differ in grammatical type (declarative vs. performative), which prevents their conjunction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the conjunction
Read the Arabic sentence beginning with 'وإنما لم يأتِ بحرف العطف' and its translation. Notice the causal explanations after إشارة or احتمال.
2
Identify the first reason
The first possibility is that the omission indicates that each of the basmalah and hamdalah is sufficient to achieve the intended purpose of beginning.
3
Identify the second reason
The second possibility is grammatical: one may be declarative (khabariyya) and the other performative (insha'iyya), and conjoining them is not allowed.
The correct view is that conjunction of these types is impermissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (الحَمْدُ اللهِ ... إلى آخره قال النووي رحمه الله تعالى : (يستحبُّ الحمدُ في ابتداء الكتب المصنفة، وكذا في ابتداء دروس المدرسين، وقراءة الطالبين بين يدي المعلمين، سواءٌ قرأ حديثاً أو فقهاً أو غيرَهُما ، وأحسنُ العبارات في ذلك : الحمد لله رب العالمين) انتهى (١). وإنما لم يأتِ بحرف العطف إشارة إلى أن كلا من البسملة والحمدلة محصل للمقصود في الابتداء، أو لاحتمال أن تكون إحداهما خبرية والأخرى إنشائية، والصحيحُ أنه لا يجوز عطفُ الإنشاء على الإخبار وعكسه.
His saying: 'Praise belongs to God... etc.' Al-Nawawi (may God have mercy on him) said: 'It is recommended to praise God at the beginning of authored books, and likewise at the beginning of teachers' lessons, and the reading of students in front of teachers, whether it is a hadith or fiqh or other, and the best expressions for that is: Praise be to God, Lord of the worlds.' End. And he (the author) did not come with a conjunction indicating that each of the basmala and hamdalah achieves the intended purpose in the beginning, or because one of them may be declarative and the other performative, and the correct view is that it is not permissible to conjoin the performative to the declarative or vice versa.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage begins with the author commenting on the opening verse of the poem: "الحمد لله". He quotes Imam al-Nawawi to establish the recommendation (istihbab) of beginning books and lessons with praise. Then he states that the author of the poem omitted the conjunction between the basmala and the hamdala, indicating that either one suffices for the purpose of beginning. Al-Sanusi then explains that the reason for the lack of conjunction could also be grammatical: one expression is declarative (khabariyya) and the other is performative (insha'iyya), and it is not allowed to conjoin a performative to a declarative or vice versa.

Key Terms

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the omission of conjunction is a grammatical mistake or oversight.
Correction: The passage explains that the omission is intentional, either to indicate sufficiency of each phrase or due to grammatical constraints preventing conjunction of different sentence types.

Checkpoint

A) Because it is forbidden to mention them together
B) To show that either one suffices for the opening, and because they differ in grammatical type
C) Because the basmalah is not considered a complete sentence
D) Because the hamdalah is not recommended at the beginning

Key Takeaway
The omission of conjunction between basmalah and hamdalah is intentional and justified both by sufficiency of each phrase and by the grammatical rule against conjoining declarative and performative sentences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author teaches that a performative and a declarative sentence cannot be conjoined. Examine your own speech: do you mix intentions when you recite formulas? How can you ensure that your starting phrase aligns with its true function—either initiating action or stating praise?

The Nine Possibilities in 'Al-Hamdu Lillah'
Enumeration of three meanings each for 'al' and 'lam' resulting in nine possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the nine possible interpretations of 'al-hamdu lillah' arise from the three meanings of 'al' and three meanings of 'lam'.

Core Idea
Three meanings of 'al' times three meanings of 'lam' equals nine interpretations.

The Basmala phrase 'al-hamdu lillah' is more than a simple greeting. Its grammatical components carry multiple possible meanings. In this lesson, we explore the linguistic possibilities that emerge from the definite article 'al' and the preposition 'lam' in 'lillah', setting the stage for a deeper theological analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage carefully to identify the three meanings of 'al' and the three meanings of 'lam'.
Notice that the author lists them in order.
2
Understand each meaning
For 'al': istighraq (universality), jins (genus), 'ahd (covenant). For 'lam': istihqaq (entitlement), ikhtisas (specification), milk (possession).
These are technical terms in Arabic grammar and theology.
3
Multiply to get nine
Since each choice for 'al' can combine with each choice for 'lam', there are 3 × 3 = 9 possible interpretations.
This multiplication is explicitly mentioned in the text.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why knowing these possibilities is important for interpreting the phrase correctly in theological context.
This will lead to the next lesson on the impossible combination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن (أل) في الحمد إما للاستغراق، أو للجنس، أو للعهد، واللام : في (الله) إما للاستحقاق، أو للاختصاص، أو للملك ; فيتحصَّلُ من ذلك احتمالات تسعة قائمة من ضرب ثلاثة في ثلاثة، يمتنع منها جعل اللام للملك مع جعل (أل) للعهد إذا جُعل المعهود هو الحمد القديم فقط ; لأن القديم لا يُملكُ، بخلاف ما إذا جُعل المعهودُ حمدَ مَنْ يُعتد بحمده; كحمده تعالى وحمد أنبيائه وأوليائه وأصفيائه; لأن المعهود حينئذ هو الجملة المركبة من القديم والحادث، والقاعدة: أن المركَّبَ من القديم والحادث حادث; فيصح أن يملك.
Then know that 'al' in 'al-hamdu' is either for universality (istighraq), or for genus (jins), or for covenant ('ahd). And 'lam' in 'lillah' is either for entitlement (istihqaq), or for specification (ikhtisas), or for possession (milk). So from that are obtained nine probabilities resulting from multiplying three by three. Among them is impossible: making the 'lam' for possession with making 'al' for covenant if the covenanted is only the eternal praise, because the eternal is not possessed. Contrary to if the covenanted is made the praise of those whose praise is regarded, such as His praise and the praise of His prophets, His saints, and His chosen ones. Because then the covenanted is a composite of the eternal and the contingent. And the rule is: the composite of the eternal and the contingent is contingent, so it is valid to possess it.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and theological analysis. The author notes that the definite article 'al' in 'al-hamdu' has three possible meanings: universality (encompassing all praise), genus (referring to the kind of praise), or covenant (a specific known praise). The prepositional phrase 'lillah' has the letter 'lam' which can mean entitlement (deserving), specification (exclusively for), or possession (ownership). Multiplying these gives nine combinations. One combination is invalid: if 'lam' means possession and 'al' means covenant, but the covenanted praise is only the eternal praise (God's praise of Himself), because the eternal cannot be owned. However, if the covenanted praise includes both eternal and contingent praise (e.g., praise of prophets and saints), then the composite is contingent and can be possessed. This shows the subtlety of interpreting the phrase.

Key Terms
استغراق (istighraq - universality)
جنس (jins - genus)
عهد ('ahd - covenant)
استحقاق (istihqaq - entitlement)
اختصاص (ikhtisas - specification)
ملك (milk - possession)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'al' always functions as a definite article without further nuance.
Correction: In theological and grammatical analysis, 'al' can indicate universality, genus, or a specific known entity (covenant).

Checkpoint
How many possible interpretations of 'al-hamdu lillah' arise from the combination of the meanings of 'al' and 'lam'?
A) Six
B) Nine
C) Three
D) Twelve

Key Takeaway
Nine possible interpretations of 'al-hamdu lillah' emerge from the three meanings of 'al' and three meanings of 'lam'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'al-hamdu lillah', which of the nine combinations resonates most with your current state of gratitude? Are you acknowledging all praise belongs to Allah (istighraq + istihqaq), or is there a specific blessing on your mind?
?
The grammatical possibilities show that praising Allah is not a casual act but layered in meaning. Does your daily recitation of the Fatiha reflect this depth, or have you become mechanical? What would it take for your heart to engage with every possibility?
?
If you were to choose only one of the nine meanings to meditate on for the next week, which would it be and why? How would that choice change your relationship with the words you say in prayer?

The Impossible Combination: Possession of the Eternal
Identification and reasoning for one impossible combination among the nine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why combining 'lam' for possession and 'al' for covenant is invalid when the covenanted praise is only eternal, and why it becomes valid if the covenanted includes contingent praise.

Core Idea
The eternal cannot be possessed, making one combination invalid.

Among the nine possible interpretations of 'al-hamdu lillah', one is logically invalid. This lesson examines that impossible combination: when 'lam' indicates possession and 'al' indicates a covenant referring only to eternal praise. The reason is rooted in the theological principle that the eternal cannot be owned. However, if the covenanted praise includes both eternal and contingent elements, the combination becomes valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the impossible combination
Read the passage to find which combination is said to be impossible: 'lam' for possession with 'al' for covenant under a specific condition.
The condition is that the covenanted praise is only the eternal praise.
2
Understand the reason
The text explains that the eternal cannot be possessed, so the combination is invalid.
This is a key theological rule: the eternal is not subject to ownership.
3
Explore the exception
If the covenanted praise includes both eternal and contingent praise (e.g., the praise of prophets and saints), then the composite is contingent and can be possessed.
The rule: a composite of eternal and contingent is contingent.
4
Apply the reasoning
Consider how this analysis shows the careful consideration of linguistic and theological principles in interpreting 'al-hamdu lillah'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن (أل) في الحمد إما للاستغراق، أو للجنس، أو للعهد، واللام : في (الله) إما للاستحقاق، أو للاختصاص، أو للملك ; فيتحصَّلُ من ذلك احتمالات تسعة قائمة من ضرب ثلاثة في ثلاثة، يمتنع منها جعل اللام للملك مع جعل (أل) للعهد إذا جُعل المعهود هو الحمد القديم فقط ; لأن القديم لا يُملكُ، بخلاف ما إذا جُعل المعهودُ حمدَ مَنْ يُعتد بحمده; كحمده تعالى وحمد أنبيائه وأوليائه وأصفيائه; لأن المعهود حينئذ هو الجملة المركبة من القديم والحادث، والقاعدة: أن المركَّبَ من القديم والحادث حادث; فيصح أن يملك.
Then know that 'al' in 'al-hamdu' is either for universality (istighraq), or for genus (jins), or for covenant ('ahd). And 'lam' in 'lillah' is either for entitlement (istihqaq), or for specification (ikhtisas), or for possession (milk). So from that are obtained nine probabilities resulting from multiplying three by three. Among them is impossible: making the 'lam' for possession with making 'al' for covenant if the covenanted is only the eternal praise, because the eternal is not possessed. Contrary to if the covenanted is made the praise of those whose praise is regarded, such as His praise and the praise of His prophets, His saints, and His chosen ones. Because then the covenanted is a composite of the eternal and the contingent. And the rule is: the composite of the eternal and the contingent is contingent, so it is valid to possess it.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and theological analysis. The author notes that the definite article 'al' in 'al-hamdu' has three possible meanings: universality (encompassing all praise), genus (referring to the kind of praise), or covenant (a specific known praise). The prepositional phrase 'lillah' has the letter 'lam' which can mean entitlement (deserving), specification (exclusively for), or possession (ownership). Multiplying these gives nine combinations. One combination is invalid: if 'lam' means possession and 'al' means covenant, but the covenanted praise is only the eternal praise (God's praise of Himself), because the eternal cannot be owned. However, if the covenanted praise includes both eternal and contingent praise (e.g., praise of prophets and saints), then the composite is contingent and can be possessed. This shows the subtlety of interpreting the phrase.

Key Terms
قديم (qadim - eternal)
حادث (hadith - contingent)
مركب (murakkab - composite)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The combination of possession and covenant is always invalid.
Correction: It is only invalid when the covenanted praise is exclusively eternal; if it includes contingent praise, the combination is valid.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The combination of possession and covenant is impossible when the covenanted is solely eternal, because the eternal cannot be owned; but it becomes possible if the covenanted includes contingent praise, making the composite contingent.

The Meaning and Superiority of Restricted Praise
Explanation of the phrase 'ala salatihi' and defense of restricted praise

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the phrase 'ala salatihi' in the poem, the grammatical reasoning behind its separation, and why restricted praise is considered superior to absolute praise in this context.

Core Idea
Restricted praise is superior because it is a direct response to Allah's blessings, earning the reward of an obligatory act.

This lesson unpacks the phrase 'ala salatihi' from the poem. The author first clarifies the grammatical relationship and the meaning of 'silaat', then presents a theological argument for why praising Allah specifically for His blessings (restricted praise) is better than absolute praise. This discussion touches on grammar, semantics, and Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic once without overthinking. Then read the English translation to capture the full argument.
2
Analyze the grammar of 'ala salatihi'
Notice that 'ala' is for causation, and the phrase is attached to 'al-hamd'. The separation between the masdar and its ma'mul is excused here.
Some scholars consider it a second khabar, leading to two praises.
3
Understand the meaning of 'silaat'
Silaat means gifts. The author explains two possible meanings: the thing given or the act of giving. He favors the act of giving as it is a direct praise of God's attribute.
4
Learn the superiority of restricted praise
The author argues that praising God specifically for His blessings is better because it is an act of gratitude rewarded as an obligation, like repaying a debt.
There is a difference of opinion; some say absolute praise is better.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( على صِلَاتِهِ ) أي : لأجل صلاته ; فـ ( على ) للتعليل; على حد قوله تعالى: وَلِتُكَبِرُوا اللَّهَ عَلَى مَا هَدَنَكُمْ [البقرة: ۱۸۵] ، والجار والمجرور متعلق بالحمد. واغتُفرَ الفصلُ بين المصدر ومعمولِهِ بالخبر لأن ذلك يُغتفر في الجار والمجرور، وبعضُهم جعله خبراً بعد خبر، فيكون المصنِّفُ قد حمد أوَّلاً في مقابلة الذات، ثم حمد ثانياً في مقابلة الصلات. ثم إن الصلات - بكسر الصاد - : جمع صلة ; وهي العطيَّة; بمعنى الشيء المُعطَى كما هو المتبادر، أو بمعنى الإعطاء، وهو أولى; لأنه حمدٌ على صفة المولى بلا واسطة والحمدُ على الشيء المعطى حمدٌ على الصفة بواسطة; وإنما اختار الحمد المقيَّد دون المطلق ، لأن المقيَّد أفضل من المطلق ; فإنه يُثاب على المقيَّد ثواب الواجب ; لكونه في مقابلة نعمة، فهو كأداء الديون ، وبعضُهم ذهب إلى أن المطلق أفضلُ .
His saying: 'upon His communications' means: for the sake of His communications; so 'ala' is for causation, like His statement: 'and that you magnify Allah for that which He has guided you' (Q. 2:185). The prepositional phrase is related to 'al-hamd'. The separation between the verbal noun (masdar) and its object (ma'mul) by the predicate (khabar) is excused because that is excused in the case of a prepositional phrase. And some made it a predicate after a predicate, so the author (al-musannif) first praised in exchange for the Essence, then praised a second time in exchange for the communications (silaat). Then 'silaat' (with kasra on the sad) is the plural of 'sila', which is a gift, either meaning the thing given (as is apparent) or the act of giving. The latter is better because it is praise upon the attribute of the Master without mediation, while praise upon the thing given is praise upon the attribute with mediation. He only chose the restricted praise (al-hamd al-muqayyad) rather than the absolute (al-mutlaq) because the restricted is better than the absolute; for one is rewarded for the restricted with the reward of the obligatory (wajib) because it is in exchange for a blessing, so it is like the payment of debts. And some held that the absolute is better.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage explains the phrase 'ala salatihi' from the poem. The author clarifies that 'ala' means 'for the sake of' (causation), quoting a Quranic verse. The phrase 'ala salatihi' is related to 'al-hamd'. A grammatical issue: the ma'mul (object of the masdar) is separated from the masdar by the khabar 'al-hamdu lillah', but this is permissible with prepositional phrases. Some grammarians consider 'ala salatihi' as a second khabar, resulting in two praises: one for the Essence and one for the attributes. Then the word 'silaat' (communications) is explained: it is the plural of 'sila' meaning gift. Two interpretations: the thing given, or the act of giving. The act of giving is better because it is praising God's attribute directly, whereas praising the thing given is indirect. Finally, the author argues that restricted praise (specifically for blessings) is superior to absolute praise because it is an obligatory act of gratitude, like repaying a debt. However, some scholars hold that absolute praise is superior.

مصدر
معمول
خبر
حمد مقيد
حمد مطلق
واجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Absolute praise is always better than restricted praise.
Correction: The author shows that restricted praise can be better because it is a direct response to a blessing and earns the reward of an obligatory act.

Checkpoint

A) Because it is more eloquent
B) Because it includes the name of Allah
C) Because it is like repaying a debt and earns reward of obligatory acts
D) Because it is longer

Key Takeaway
Restricted praise for Allah's blessings is superior to absolute praise because it fulfills the obligation of gratitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Wisdom of Restricted Praise in 'Upon His Gifts'
Commenting on the grammar and meaning of 'upon His gifts' and the preference for restricted praise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical and theological reasoning behind the author's choice of restricted praise over absolute praise in the phrase 'upon His gifts.'

Core Idea
Restricted praise is better because it directly acknowledges Allah's generosity without intermediary.

This lesson examines the author's deliberate choice of the phrase 'upon His gifts.' We explore the grammatical meaning of 'upon' as a particle of causation, the dual meaning of 'sila', and why praising for the act of giving is superior to praising for the thing given. The discussion also contrasts restricted and absolute praise, clarifying a nuanced point in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic Passage and Translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its literal translation. Notice the technical terms and the flow of the commentary.
The passage includes footnotes which are part of the original text.
2
Understand the Grammatical Role of 'ala'
Identify why 'ala' (upon) is interpreted as a particle of causation. Relate it to the Quranic verse cited.
This grammatical point is crucial for the meaning of the phrase.
3
Distinguish Between Two Meanings of 'sila'
Clarify that 'sila' can mean either 'the thing given' or 'the act of giving'. Note the commentator's preference and the reason.
This distinction affects whether praise is direct or indirect.
4
Grasp Why Restricted Praise is Preferred
Understand the argument that the restricted praise (upon His gifts) is like an obligatory duty, while absolute praise is supererogatory. The analogy of debt repayment is key.
The commentator contrasts this with the view of some scholars who prefer absolute praise.
5
Reflect on the Analogy and Broader Implications
Consider how this discussion illustrates the importance of linguistic precision in theological discourse and the balance between gratitude and praise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( على صلاته ) أي : لأجل صلاته ؛ فـ ( على ) للتعليل؛ على حد قوله تعالى: وَلِتُكَبِرُوا اللَّهَ عَلَى مَا هَدَنَكُمْ ﴾ [البقرة: ۱۸۵] ، والجار والمجرور متعلق بالحمد. واغتُفرَ الفصلُ بين المصدر ومعمولِهِ بالخبر (۳) لأن ذلك يُغتفر في الجار والمجرور، وبعضُهم جعله خبراً بعد خبر، فيكون المصنِّفُ قد حمد أوَّلاً في مقابلة الذات، ثم حمد ثانياً في مقابلة الصلات. ثم إن الصلات - بكسر الصاد - : جمع صلة ؛ وهي العطيَّة؛ بمعنى الشيء المُعطَى كما هو المتبادر، أو بمعنى الإعطاء، وهو أولى؛ لأنه حمدٌ على صفة المولى بلا واسطة والحمدُ على الشيء المعطى حمدٌ على الصفة بواسطة؛ وإنما اختار الحمد المقيَّد دون المطلق (٤) ، لأن المقيَّد أفضل من المطلق ؛ فإنه يُثاب على المقيَّد ثواب الواجب ؛ لكونه في مقابلة نعمة، فهو كأداء الديون (٥) ، وبعضُهم ذهب إلى أن المطلق أفضل . (۱) وإنما جعله معتدا به كهذا الحمد الذي لا شكّ في كماله .. ليكون معهوداً، فلو قلنا : أي حمد .. تعيَّن جعل (أل) للاستغراق أو للجنس، والقديم هنا : هو حمد الله لنفسه سبحانه، والحادث هنا : هو حمد أنبيائه له جل وعز. (۲) والتركيب هنا ذهني كما لا يخفى وليس تركيباً حقيقياً ؛ لاستحالته، فلذلك كان حادثاً، أو قل: هو اعتباري يختلف شأنه باختلاف التعلَّق، وعليه فهو قديم باعتبار وحادث باعتبار. (۳) المصدر هنا : الحمد ومعموله على صلاته والخبر الله . (٤) قوله : (المطلق) المراد بالمطلق ما كان في مقابلة جميل غير نعمة وليس المراد به ما لا مقابل له؛ لأن حقيقة الحمد الثناء لأجل جميل اختياري، فلا بد له من مقابل. (٥) وجه التشبيه هنا كونه واجب الأداء، والواجب - فيما عدا ما استثني - أفضل من النفل .
His saying: 'upon His gifts' means: for the sake of His gifts; so 'upon' is for causation, on the order of His saying, the Exalted: 'and to magnify Allah for His guidance of you' [al-Baqarah 185], and the prepositional phrase is attached to 'praise'. The separation between the verbal noun and its object by the predicate is overlooked (3) because that is overlooked in the case of the prepositional phrase, and some have made it a second predicate, so the author praised first in return for the essence, then praised second in return for the gifts. Then 'al-silat' - with kasra on the sad - is a plural of 'sila'; and it is the gift; in the sense of the thing given as is apparent, or in the sense of giving, and it is preferable; because it is praise upon the attribute of the Mawla without intermediary, while praise upon the thing given is praise upon the attribute with an intermediary; and he only chose the restricted praise over the absolute (4), because the restricted is better than the absolute; for one is rewarded for the restricted the reward of an obligation; because it is in return for a blessing, so it is like the payment of debts (5), and some have held that the absolute is better. (1) And he only made it considered like this praise which has no doubt in its perfection... to be something well-known, so if we said: any praise... then making 'al' for generality or genus is determined, and the eternal here is the praise of Allah for Himself, and the temporal here is the praise of His prophets for Him. (2) And the composition here is mental, as is not hidden, and it is not a real composition; due to its impossibility, therefore it was temporal, or you say: it is relational, its case varies according to the relation, and accordingly it is eternal in one respect and temporal in another. (3) The verbal noun here is 'praise' and its object 'upon His gifts' and the predicate 'Allah'. (4) His saying: (the absolute) what is meant by the absolute is that which is in return for a beautiful [act] that is not a blessing, and what is meant is not that which has no counterpart; because the reality of praise is commendation for a volitional beautiful [act], so it must have a counterpart. (5) The similarity here is that it is obligatory to fulfill, and the obligatory - except what is excluded - is better than the supererogatory.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is a detailed grammatical and theological commentary on the author's phrase 'al-hamdu lillah 'ala salatih' (Praise be to Allah upon His gifts). The commentator explains that 'ala' here denotes causation, meaning the praise is for the sake of the gifts. He notes that the prepositional phrase is attached to 'al-hamd' (praise), and the separation between the verbal noun and its object by the predicate is permissible. Some scholars consider the phrase a second predicate, giving the sense that the author first praised in return for the Divine Essence (in the phrase 'al-hamdu lillah'), then praised in return for the gifts. The commentator then clarifies that 'al-silat' means gifts, either the things given or the act of giving. He prefers the latter because it allows praise for Allah's attribute of generosity directly, without the intermediary of the created gift. This leads to a discussion of why the author chose the restricted praise (i.e., 'on His gifts') over absolute praise (praise without specification). The restricted praise is considered better because it corresponds to the obligatory gratitude owed for a specific blessing, akin to paying a debt, whereas absolute praise is supererogatory. The footnote (4) defines 'absolute' as praise in return for a beautiful act that is not a blessing, emphasizing that praise always requires a counterpart. Footnotes (1)-(3) and (5) provide further technical refinements about the definite article, the mental composition of eternal and temporal, and the analogy with debts.

العطية
الإعطاء
الحمد المقيَّد
الحمد المطلق
الواجب
النفل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Absolute praise is always superior to restricted praise.
Correction: In the context of gratitude for specific blessings, restricted praise is better because it corresponds to an obligatory duty, whereas absolute praise is supererogatory.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator prefer the meaning of 'al-silat' as 'giving' rather than 'the thing given'?
A) Because 'giving' is a verbal noun and thus easier to parse.
B) Because praising the act of giving is a direct praise of the attribute of generosity, whereas praising the thing given is indirect.
C) Because the thing given is created and temporal, while giving is eternal.
D) Because the author explicitly states that 'giving' is the intended meaning.

Key Takeaway
The choice of restricted praise in the phrase 'upon His gifts' emphasizes gratitude for specific blessings, which is obligatory and thus more excellent than absolute praise in this context.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'al-hamdu lillah', do you often stay in the comfort of general praise, or do you deliberately recall specific blessings that demand a response of gratitude? Which blessings have you never truly thanked Him for with focused praise?

The Grammar of 'Thumma' and the Order of Rank
Grammatical analysis of 'thumma' and the hierarchy of Creator over creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possible grammatical roles of the particle 'thumma' in this context and why the author's order reflects a hierarchical ranking of divine and prophetic attributes.

Core Idea
The particle 'thumma' signals a ranking of praise, first the Creator then the Prophet.

In this passage, the commentator pauses on the word 'thumma' (then) to explore its grammatical function. He considers whether it is a new sentence or a conjunction, and if conjunction, whether it indicates mere sequence or an order of rank. This analysis reveals a deeper theological point: the Prophet's peace and prayer are secondary to the divine attributes mentioned earlier.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on 'thumma'
Read the Arabic carefully and its translation. Note the two possibilities: isti'naf (resumption) and 'atf (conjunction).
The commentator presents these as hypothetical interpretations.
2
Understand the two types of conjunction
If conjunction, it could be for sequential mention (dhikri) or sequential rank (rutabi). The commentator prefers rank-based order because the Prophet's affairs are lower than Allah's.
This shows the poem's arrangement reflects theological hierarchy.
3
Reflect on the rank of the Prophet
The passage states that matters related to the created being (Prophet) are later and distant from matters related to the Creator. Consider why this matters in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ثُمَّ سَلَامُ اللهِ ... إلى آخره يحتمل أن تكون (ثم) للاستئناف، ويحتمل أن تكون للعطف ؛ وعلى الثاني : فيحتمل أن تكون للترتيب الذكري، وأن تكون للترتيب الرتبي ؛ لأن رتبةً ما يتعلق بالمخلوق من الصلاة والسلام متأخرة ومتراخية عن رتبة ما يتعلَّق بالخالق من البسملة والحمدلة. ومعنى (سلام الله أي تحيَّتُه اللائقة به و بحسب ما عنده تعالى، كما تشعر به إضافته له تعالى، فالمطلوب تحيةٌ عظمى بلغت الدرجة القصوى، فتكون أعظم التحيات؛ لأنه أعظم المخلوقات؛ والمراد بالتحية في حقه صلى الله عليه وسلم كما أفاده السنوسي في شرح الجزائرية»: أن يُسمِعَهُ كلامَهُ القديم الدال على رفعة مقامِهِ العظيم ولم يرتض بعضُهم تفسير (السلام) بالأمن وإن ذكره السنوسي وغيره (۳) ؛ لأنه ربما أشعرَ بمَظنَّة الخوف مع أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم - بل وأتباعه - لا خوف عليهم، نعم يخاف صلى الله عليه وسلم خوف مهابة وإجلال، ولذلك قال : «إنِّي لأخوفُكُمْ مِنَ اللهِ (٤) . (۲) فإن قيل : إن السلام يؤخَّرُ عن الصلاة كما جرى به عرف الاستعمال ؛ لآية : (يناها الذِينَ ءَامَنُوا صَلُّوا عَلَيْهِ وَسَلِمُوا تَسْلِيمًا [الأحزاب: ٥٦] ، فما بال المصنفِ قدَّمَهُ عنها ؟ أجيب بأن ذلك لضرورة النظم () ، على أنه أشار بلطف إلى أن رتبته التأخيرُ؛ حيث أدخل (مع) على الصلاة، وهي تدخل على المتبوع، يقال: جاء الوزير مع السلطان، دون العكس. قوله : (مَعْ صَلَاتِهِ) بإسكان العين هنا على اللغة القليلة لأجل الوزن، وإن كان الأفصح فتحها . ومعنى (صلاته) رحمته المقرونة بالتعظيم كما تشعر به الإضافة إلى ضميره. (۱) في :(ب): (فالمطلق) بدل (فالمطلوب). (۲) انظر: «شرح الجزائرية (ص) (۳۵) ، ونقله العلامة الأمير في حاشيته» (ص ۱۰ - ۱۱ ) . (۳) المصدر السابق (ص ۳۵)، ورجحه السنوسي فيها على التحية. (٤) أخرج البخاري (٥٠٦٣) بلفظ : «إني لأخشاكم الله». (٥) في (ب) (الموافقة الشطرة الأولى; حيث قال : «على صلاته).
His saying: 'then the peace of Allah... to the end' it is possible that 'thumma' is for resumption, and it is possible that it is for conjunction; and on the second: it is possible that it is for sequential mention, and that it is for sequential rank; because the rank of what pertains to the created being of prayer and peace is later and distant from the rank of what pertains to the Creator of the Basmala and Hamdala. And the meaning of 'the peace of Allah' is His greeting befitting Him and according to what He has, as suggested by its attribution to Him, so what is sought is a supreme greeting that has reached the utmost degree, so it becomes the greatest of greetings; because he is the greatest of creatures; and what is meant by greeting in his right, peace be upon him, as Sanusi stated in his commentary on al-Jaza'iriyya: that He makes him hear His eternal speech indicating the eminence of his great station. And some did not approve of interpreting 'al-salam' as security, even though Sanusi and others mentioned it (3); because it might suggest the possibility of fear, while the Prophet - peace be upon him - and even his followers have no fear on them, yes, the Prophet fears a fear of awe and veneration, and therefore he said: 'I am the most fearing of you of Allah' (4). (2) If it is said: peace is delayed after prayer as is customary in usage; because of the verse: 'O you who believe, send blessings upon him and greet him with a greeting of peace' [al-Ahzab 56], so why did the author place it before it? The answer is that this is due to poetic necessity, though he subtly indicated that its rank is delay; since he inserted 'ma'a' upon prayer, and it enters upon the principal, one says: 'the vizier came with the sultan', not the reverse. His saying: 'with His prayer' with the 'ayn made silent here due to the rarer dialect for the sake of meter, although the more elegant is to open it. And the meaning of 'His prayer' is His mercy accompanied by veneration, as suggested by its attribution to His pronoun. (1) In (b): 'al-mutlaq' instead of 'al-matloob'. (2) See: 'Sharh al-Jaza'iriyya' (p. 35), and al-Amir transmitted it in his gloss (p. 10-11). (3) Same source (p. 35), and Sanusi preferred it over greeting in it. (4) Bukhari (5063) with the wording: 'I am the most fearing of you of Allah'. (5) In (b): accordance with the first hemistich; where he said: 'upon His prayers'.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part commentary on the author's phrase 'thumma salam Allah... ma'a salatih' (then the peace of Allah... with His prayer). First, the commentator analyzes the particle 'thumma' (then): it could be for resumption (starting a new sentence) or conjunction (showing sequence). If conjunction, it could be for order of mention or for order of rank. He prefers the latter because the rank of matters related to the created (the Prophet's salat and salam) is lower and comes after matters related to the Creator (Basmala and Hamdala). Second, he explains 'salam Allah' as Allah's greeting specific to the Prophet, being the greatest greeting because he is the greatest creature. He cites Sanusi that it means hearing Allah's eternal speech. He notes a disagreement with interpreting 'salam' as 'security' because the Prophet has no fear; rather, his fear is of awe. Third, he addresses an objection: why does the author mention salam before salat, when the verse and custom place salat first? The answer is poetic necessity ('darurat al-nazm'), and the author subtly indicates the actual order by using 'ma'a' (with) before salat, which implies that salat is the principal element, just as we say 'the vizier came with the sultan' (the vizier is secondary). Thus, the word 'ma'a' hints that salat should come first in rank. Fourth, he comments on the pronunciation of 'ma'a' with sukun on the 'ayn for meter. Finally, he defines 'salat' as mercy coupled with veneration, as indicated by the pronoun attribution (i.e., 'His prayer' means Allah's mercy toward the Prophet). The footnotes provide variant readings and sources.

Key Terms
ثم
الاستئناف
العطف
الترتيب الذكري
الترتيب الرتبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what does 'thumma' primarily indicate in this line?
A) Resumption (isti'naf)
B) Sequential rank (tartib rutabi)
C) Sequential mention (tartib dhikri)
D) Both B and C are equally possible

Key Takeaway
The word 'thumma' in the poem indicates that the Prophet's rank, though great, is secondary to Allah's.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite blessings upon the Prophet, do you consciously start with praising Allah first? If not, what does that reveal about the true order of your heart's devotion? Starting today, commit to beginning every salawat with a short praise of Allah before sending blessings on the Prophet.
?
Where in your life have you placed love for the Prophet—or any created thing—above your obedience to Allah? Think of one specific habit or attachment that violates this hierarchy. What concrete step will you take tonight to restore Allah to His rightful first place?
?
This passage teaches that the Prophet's rank is secondary to Allah's, even in poetic praise. Does your own speech reflect this rank? When you speak about your love for the Prophet, do you inadvertently elevate him to a level that only Allah deserves? How will you adjust your words and thoughts to ensure you give Allah the precedence He commands?

The Supreme Greeting: Meaning of 'Salam Allah'
Explanation of 'salam Allah' as a supreme greeting and refutation of the 'security' interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of 'salam Allah' as a supreme greeting specific to the Prophet, and the debate over whether it implies 'security'.

Core Idea
The peace of Allah upon the Prophet is the greatest greeting, not mere security.

The commentator now explains what 'salam Allah' means. He defines it as a greeting befitting Allah, and thus the highest form of praise for the Prophet, who is the greatest creature. He then addresses a disagreement: some interpret 'salam' as 'security' (amn), but the commentator rejects this view because the Prophet has no fear, though he does have a fear of awe.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of 'salam Allah'
Read the passage and translation. Note that it is described as a greeting (tahiyya) that is supreme due to its attribution to Allah.
The commentator cites Sanusi's explanation.
2
Understand the 'security' interpretation
Some scholars interpret 'salam' as 'security' from fear. The commentator gives reasons why this is problematic: the Prophet has no fear, but he does have a reverential fear of Allah.
The hadith 'I am the most fearing of you of Allah' is cited.
3
Reflect on the rank of the Prophet
Consider why the Prophet receives the greatest greeting and how this connects to his status as the greatest creature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Your fear of Allah: is it primarily fear of consequences, or is it the awe that comes from recognizing His infinite greatness? How can you cultivate the latter starting today?

Ordering of Salam and Salat: Poetic Necessity and Subtle Indication
Defense of the author's ordering of salam before salat and explanation of 'ma'a' and 'salat'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author placed salam before salat in the poem and how the word 'ma'a' subtly indicates the correct order.

Core Idea
The author prioritized salam in the poem for poetic necessity, but 'ma'a' hints that salat is actually primary.

An objection arises: the Quran and custom place salat (blessings) before salam (peace), yet the poem has salam first. The commentator answers that this is due to poetic necessity (darurat al-nazm), and the author cleverly used the word 'ma'a' (with) before salat to hint that salat is the principal element, like a vizier accompanying a sultan. The passage also explains the pronunciation and meaning of 'ma'a' and 'salat'.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic and translation. Understand the objection based on the Quranic verse (33:56).
The verse orders 'sallu' (send blessings) before 'sallimu' (greet).
2
Understand the poetic necessity argument
The commentator says the author had to adjust word order for the poem's meter and rhyme. This is a common practice in classical Arabic poetry.
The term 'darurat al-nazm' refers to poetic license.
3
Analyze the use of 'ma'a'
The author used 'ma'a' before salat. In Arabic, 'ma'a' (with) often introduces the main element when following a subordinate one, like 'the vizier came with the sultan'. This subtly shows that salat should precede salam in rank.
4
Learn the meaning of 'salat'
The commentator defines 'salat' as Allah's mercy accompanied by veneration (rahma muqarana bi al-ta'zim).
This is consistent with other Sunni interpretations.

The answer is that this is due to poetic necessity, though he subtly indicated that its rank is delay; since he inserted 'ma'a' upon prayer, and it enters upon the principal, one says: 'the vizier came with the sultan', not the reverse. His saying: 'with His prayer' with the 'ayn made silent here due to the rarer dialect for the sake of meter, although the more elegant is to open it. And the meaning of 'His prayer' is His mercy accompanied by veneration, as suggested by its attribution to His pronoun. (1) In (b): 'al-mutlaq' instead of 'al-matloob'. (2) See: 'Sharh al-Jaza'iriyya' (p. 35), and al-Amir transmitted it in his gloss (p. 10-11). (3) Same source (p. 35), and Sanusi preferred it over greeting in it. (4) Bukhari (5063) with the wording: 'I am the most fearing of you of Allah'. (5) In (b): accordance with the first hemistich; where he said: 'upon His prayers'.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part commentary on the author's phrase 'thumma salam Allah... ma'a salatih' (then the peace of Allah... with His prayer). First, the commentator analyzes the particle 'thumma' (then): it could be for resumption (starting a new sentence) or conjunction (showing sequence). If conjunction, it could be for order of mention or for order of rank. He prefers the latter because the rank of matters related to the created (the Prophet's salat and salam) is lower and comes after matters related to the Creator (Basmala and Hamdala). Second, he explains 'salam Allah' as Allah's greeting specific to the Prophet, being the greatest greeting because he is the greatest creature. He cites Sanusi that it means hearing Allah's eternal speech. He notes a disagreement with interpreting 'salam' as 'security' because the Prophet has no fear; rather, his fear is of awe. Third, he addresses an objection: why does the author mention salam before salat, when the verse and custom place salat first? The answer is poetic necessity ('darurat al-nazm'), and the author subtly indicates the actual order by using 'ma'a' (with) before salat, which implies that salat is the principal element, just as we say 'the vizier came with the sultan' (the vizier is secondary). Thus, the word 'ma'a' hints that salat should come first in rank. Fourth, he comments on the pronunciation of 'ma'a' with sukun on the 'ayn for meter. Finally, he defines 'salat' as mercy coupled with veneration, as indicated by the pronoun attribution (i.e., 'His prayer' means Allah's mercy toward the Prophet). The footnotes provide variant readings and sources.

Key Terms
صلاة
سلام
مع
رحمته
التعظيم

Misconception: Some think the author made a mistake by putting salam before salat.
Correction: The commentator explains it is poetic necessity, and the word 'ma'a' shows the correct rank.

Checkpoint
How does the author subtly indicate that salat should be considered the main element despite being mentioned second?
A) By using the word 'thumma' to show delay.
B) By placing 'ma'a' (with) before salat, which in Arabic grammar introduces the principal item.
C) By citing a hadith about the importance of salat.
D) By repeating the word 'salat' twice.

Key Takeaway
The author's placement of salam before salat is a poetic necessity, and the word 'ma'a' reveals that salat holds the higher rank.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The author subtly affirmed the truth through the word 'ma'a' despite the poetic constraint. Are there areas in your life where external pressures (social expectations, work, etc.) cause you to present things in a way that downplays your true beliefs? Write down one practical way you can, like the author, embed a subtle reminder of the truth in your daily interactions.
?

The Benefit of Prayers upon the Prophet
Commentary on the benefit of prayers and refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate about the benefit of sending prayers upon the Prophet and the correct intention for the one who prays.

Core Idea
The benefit of prayers returns to the one who prays, but not for self-gain—rather as intercession.

In this passage, the commentator discusses a key theological point: why do we send prayers upon the Prophet? Some say it benefits only the one who prays, but the commentator refines this by directing the intention towards seeking intercession.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on benefit
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, focusing on the discussion of benefit and refutation.
2
Identify the two opinions
Note the first opinion (benefit only for the one who prays) and the refutation.
3
Understand the correct intention
The commentator advises that the one praying should not observe his own benefit but should seek intercession.
4
Connect to your own practice
Reflect on how this teaching affects your own sending of prayers upon the Prophet.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل : المنفعة عائدة على المصلي ليس إلا ؛ لأنه قد أفرغت عليه الكمالات، ورُدَّ بأن ما مِنْ كمال إلا وعند الله أعلى منه والكامل يقبلُ الكمال؛ لكن لا ينبغي للمصلّي أن يلاحظ ذلك، بل يلاحظ أنه يتوسل به عند ربه في نيل مقصوده. وفي كلام المصنّف نوع من المحسنات البديعية يسمّى بالجناس المحرَّف؛ وهو ما تماثل ركناه في الحروف لا في الحركات؛ فإنه عبّر أولاً بصلاته بكسر الصاد، ثم عبر بصلاته بفتحها، وفي هذا البيت مع ما بعده التضمين؛ وهو كما في شرح شيخ الإسلام على الخزرجية: تعلُّقُ قافية البيت بما بعدها، وهو مغتفر للمولدين عند بعضهم. وإثباتُ الصلاة والسلام في صدر الكتب والرسائل حدث في زمن ولاية بني هاشم، ثم مضى العمل على استحبابه ومن العلماء من يختم بهما الكتاب أيضاً كما في شرح المصنف الصغير.
It was said: The benefit is only returning to the one who prays, because perfections have been poured upon him. And it was refuted that there is no perfection except that Allah has what is higher than it, and the perfect one accepts perfection; but it is not appropriate for the one praying to observe that, rather he observes that he seeks intercession with his Lord in attaining his aim. And in the author's words there is a type of badi‘ (rhetorical) embellishment called altered paronomasia (al-jinas al-muharraf), which is when the two pillars are similar in letters but not in vowels; for he first expressed it with his prayer (bi salatihi) with a kasra on the sad, then expressed it with his prayer with a fatha. And in this line with what follows there is at-tadmin, which is, as in the commentary of Shaykh al-Islam on al-Khazrajiyya: the connection of the line's rhyme with what follows it, and it is excusable for the later poets according to some of them. And establishing prayer and peace at the beginning of books and letters occurred in the time of the dominion of Banu Hashim, then practice continued on its recommendation, and among scholars are those who conclude the book with them as well, as in the explanation of al-Musannaf al-Saghir.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line of poetry from Jawharat al-Tawhid, discussing two issues: the benefit of sending prayers upon the Prophet and the historical practice of writing prayers at the beginning of books. First, the author presents an opinion that the benefit of sending prayers returns only to the one who prays because perfections are bestowed upon him. This is then refuted by noting that any perfection a person attains is still less than what Allah has, and the one who is perfect (the Prophet) accepts perfection but should not observe his own benefit. Instead, the one praying should aim to seek intercession through the Prophet. The author adds a rhetorical note about the poet's use of altered paronomasia (الجناس المحرف) in the original line—the word 'صلاته' appears first with a kasra and then with a fatha. He also mentions the device of 'التضمين' (enjambment) where the rhyme of a line connects to the next line. Second, the author discusses the historical origin of writing 'الصلاة والسلام' at the beginning of books and letters, which began in the era of Banu Hashim (the Abbasids), and then became a recommended practice. Some scholars also end their books with these prayers. The commentary follows the author's order, first explaining the benefit and refutation, then the rhetorical devices, then the historical practice.

Key Terms
الصلاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The benefit of prayers upon the Prophet is only for the one who prays in a self-serving way.
Correction: The one praying should not observe his own benefit but should seek intercession with Allah through the Prophet.

Checkpoint
What should the one praying observe according to the commentator?
A) His own perfection
B) Seeking intercession with his Lord
C) The benefit returning to him
D) The Prophet's need for prayers

Key Takeaway
The one who sends prayers upon the Prophet should seek intercession with Allah, not focus on his own benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you send prayers upon the Prophet, what is the deepest intention behind your recitation? Is it merely a habit or a felt need to draw closer to Allah? Will you examine your intention right now?
?
The passage warns against observing one's own benefit. Think of a time you performed an act of worship hoping for personal gain—status, peace, or reward. How can you shift your intention to pure intercession through the Prophet?
?
If your only goal in salawat is self-betterment, how does that compare to seeking Allah's favor through the Prophet's intercession? Which do you truly want, and what will you change in your practice today?

Rhetorical Devices and History of Writing Prayers in Books
Commentary on rhetorical devices and historical practice of writing prayers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rhetorical devices of altered paronomasia and enjambment, and the historical origin of writing prayers at the beginning of books.

Core Idea
Rhetorical devices enhance the text, and the practice of beginning books with prayers traces back to the Abbasid era.

This passage explores two rhetorical figures in the poet's line and then provides a historical note on when it became customary to write prayers at the start of books and letters.

3
Learn the historical origin

The Meaning of 'Ala' in Salawat
Author explains the difference between 'salla' and 'da'a' regarding the particle 'ala'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the preposition 'ala' is used in blessings upon the Prophet and how it differs from its use in cursing, as well as its indication of firm establishment.

Core Idea
In salawat, 'ala' signals honor and firm establishment, not superiority.

When we send blessings upon the Prophet, we say 'salla Allahu alayhi wa sallam'. What does the word 'ala' mean here? This lesson clarifies the linguistic nuance, distinguishing this usage from cursing and explaining the underlying significance of firm establishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the contrast between 'salla alayhi' and 'da'a alayhi'.
Pay attention to the footnote from Al-Farahari.
2
Identify the key distinction
The author points out that 'salla alayhi' always conveys benefit, while 'da'a alayhi' always conveys harm. This is the core difference.
3
Understand 'ala' in blessings
The use of 'ala' in salawat indicates 'shiddat al-tamakkun' (firm establishment) – a sign of honor and closeness, not physical superiority.
Al-Farahari explains that 'ala' does not imply real domination, as in 'tawakkaltu ala Allah'.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this distinction matters: it protects the correct understanding of our prayers and avoids misunderstanding the nature of the Prophet's status.
This connects to the broader lesson on proper theological language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محلَّ ذلك ما لم يكن بعنوان الصلاة والسلام؛ للفرق الظاهر بين (صلَّى وسلم عليه) و(دعا) عليه ؛ إذ الأوَّلُ لا يُفهم منه إلا المنفعة، والثاني لا يُفهم منه إلا المضرَّةُ وأيضاً في التعبير بـ (على) إشارة إلى شدَّة التمكُن (١) . (١) وقال الفرهاري في مقدمات «النبراس»: «على» لا توجب الاستعلاء الحقيقي ؛ وإلا لم يجز قولك: توكلت على الله .
The place for that is when it is not under the heading of prayer and greeting; because of the apparent difference between (blessed and greeted him) and (cursed him); since the former is not understood except as benefit, and the latter is not understood except as harm. Also in the expression using (on) there is an indication of firm establishment (1). (1) And Al-Farahari said in the introductions of 'Al-Nibras': 'Ala' does not necessitate real superiority; otherwise it would not be permissible to say: I relied on Allah.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic clarification about the preposition 'ala' when used in the context of invoking blessings (salawat) upon the Prophet. The author states that the rule about the permissibility of 'ala' applies specifically to phrases of prayer and greeting, not to cursing. The difference is clear: 'salla wa sallama alayhi' (blessed and greeted him) only conveys benefit, while 'da'a alayhi' (cursed him) only conveys harm. Additionally, the use of 'ala' in blessings indicates firm establishment (tamakkun) and high status, not literal physical superiority. The footnote from Al-Farahari confirms that 'ala' does not imply real domination, as seen in 'tawakkaltu ala Allah' (I relied on Allah), where no physical elevation is meant.

Correction: In salawat, 'ala' indicates honor and firm establishment, as shown by the distinction from cursing and the example 'tawakkaltu ala Allah'.

Grammar Reveals the Prophet's Unique Mission
The commentator explains the grammatical function of the line describing the Prophet's unique mission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how grammatical analysis of a single poetic line identifies Prophet Muhammad as the only prophet sent when religion was devoid of monotheism.

Core Idea
A circumstantial accusative makes the description exclusive to the Prophet.

The commentator takes a line from a poem about Prophet Muhammad and dissects its grammar to highlight a profound theological truth. What seems like a simple sentence is actually a carefully crafted statement that, through Arabic grammatical rules, restricts the description of 'coming with monotheism when religion was empty' to the Prophet alone. In this lesson, we will see how the tanwin, adjective, and circumstantial accusative work together to assert the uniqueness of his mission.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context of the line
Read the line 'جاء وقد خلا الدين عن التوحيد' (He came when religion was devoid of monotheism) in its broader poetic context. Note that this line describes the Prophet's arrival at a time of spiritual void.
2
Grasp the function of tanwin on 'nabiy'
The tanwin (نون ساكنة) on the word 'نبي' (prophet) indicates both glorification and indefiniteness. The commentator says its specification comes later in the line 'محمد العاقب لرسل'.
3
Identify the adjective and circumstantial accusative
The entire clause 'جاء وقد خلا الدين عن التوحيد' is an adjective for 'nabiy', but the phrase 'وقد خلا الدين عن التوحيد' is a circumstantial accusative (حال) from the subject of 'came'. This restricts the adjective, making it exclusive to our Prophet.
This grammatical move is the key to the theological claim.
4
See the theological conclusion
Because the circumstantial accusative restricts its operator, no other prophet could be described as coming with monotheism in a state where religion was devoid of it. Thus, the line asserts the unique role of Muhammad as the reviver of pure monotheism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن التنوينَ في (نبي) للتعظيم والإبهام فيه يرفعه ما يأتي في كلامه، وهو قوله : (محمد العاقب لرسل ... إلى آخره)، بعد إن شاء الله تعالى . قوله : جاء ... إلى آخره هذه الجملة صفةٌ لنبي؛ كما هو القاعدة من أن الجمل بعد النكرات صفات، وقد قيَّد الناظم هذه الجملة بقوله : (وقد خلا الدين عن التوحيد) لأنه حال من فاعل (جاء)، والحال قيد في عاملها، فصارت الصفة بهذا الاعتبار مخصصة للموصوف وقاصرة له على نبينا الله ؛ لأنه لم يأتِ نبي بالتوحيد في حال خلو الدين عن التوحيد إلا نبينا ، والمراد بالمجيء : الإرسال، فتفسيره به تفسير مراد ؛ لأنه تفسير بالسبب، فإن الإرسال سبب للمجيء.
Know that the tanwin (تنوين) in 'nabiy' (نبي) is for glorification and vagueness, and what removes it comes later in his speech, which is his saying: 'Muhammad, the seal of the messengers ... to the end,' after God willing. His saying: 'came ... to the end' – this sentence is an adjective (صفة) for 'nabiy'; as per the rule that sentences after indefinite nouns are adjectives. And the author constrained this sentence with his saying: 'and religion had become devoid of monotheism' because it is a circumstantial accusative (حال) from the subject of 'came', and the circumstantial accusative is a restriction on its operator. Thus the adjective, by this consideration, specifies the described and restricts it to our Prophet, because no prophet came with monotheism in a state where religion was devoid of monotheism except our Prophet. And the intended meaning of 'coming' is 'sending' (إرسال), so interpreting it as such is an intended interpretation because it is an interpretation by cause, for the sending is the cause of the coming.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and theological analysis of the line from the poem: 'جاء وقد خلا الدين عن التوحيد' (He came when religion was devoid of monotheism). The commentator first explains the grammatical features of the word 'نبي' (prophet) in the poem, noting that the tanwin (the nunation) on it serves both glorification and indefiniteness, with the specification coming later in the line 'محمد العاقب لرسل' (Muhammad, the seal of the messengers). Then he examines the clause 'جاء وقد خلا الدين عن التوحيد' and states that this entire sentence is an adjective (صفة) for 'نبي' because it follows an indefinite noun, conforming to Arabic grammar where sentences after indefinite nouns are adjectives. He then points out that the author has further restricted this adjective by making the phrase 'وقد خلا الدين عن التوحيد' a circumstantial accusative (حال) from the subject of 'جاء'. In Arabic, a circumstantial accusative qualifies its operator, here the verb 'came'. By making it a حال, the description becomes exclusive: the adjective now specifies that no other prophet ever came in such a state of religious monotheistic void, thus uniquely restricting the description to the Prophet Muhammad. Finally, the commentator clarifies that 'المجيء' (coming) in this context means 'الإرسال' (sending), and that this interpretation is valid because it explains by cause: the sending is the cause of the coming. Thus, the line affirms that Muhammad was sent when the world had lost pure monotheism.

Key Terms
تنوين
صفة
حال
مجيء
إرسال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The line is merely poetic description without deeper theological significance.
Correction: The commentator demonstrates that the grammatical structure (especially the circumstantial accusative) makes the verse a precise theological statement affirming the Prophet's unique role as the restorer of monotheism.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what grammatical function does the clause 'وقد خلا الدين عن التوحيد' serve?
A) It is an adjective describing the Prophet.
B) It is a circumstantial accusative (حال) that restricts the meaning.
C) It is an independent sentence with no grammatical relation.
D) It is a predicate of an implied subject.

Key Takeaway
Through a single grammatical choice (turning a phrase into a حال), the poet and commentator affirm that Prophet Muhammad alone was sent when the world had lost all trace of monotheism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Before you were guided, was your heart not also devoid of true monotheism just as the world before the Prophet? Do you see his coming as a direct rescue from that void, and how does that urgency shape your daily devotion to him?
?
The grammatical precision of this passage shows that every detail of the Prophet's mission is intentional. Are you treating his message as replaceable or supplementary, or do you truly believe that he alone came with pure monotheism when the world needed it most? What would change in your life if you fully internalized this exclusivity?
?
If the Prophet's mission was so unique that its proof lies in a single grammatical particle, how carefully do you examine the sources of your faith? Are you willing to dive into such details to strengthen your certainty, or do you rely on superficial understanding?

The Nobility of Tawhid and the Ten Principles
Objection and answer with methodological note.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author of Jawharat al-Tawhid focuses on tawhid despite the poem covering other topics, and how the ten principles organize Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Tawhid is the noblest act of worship, but the poem's scope includes other sciences organized by ten principles.

This passage addresses a potential contradiction: the poem is titled on tawhid yet discusses other matters. The author responds by affirming tawhid's supreme rank, supported by a hadith. He also connects this to the broader methodological framework of the ten principles (mabādi‘) that every science requires, showing that even this work follows that structure.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Notice the objector's point: the poem includes non-tawhid topics, so why limit the title to tawhid? This sets up the need for justification.
2
Read the answer and hadith
The answer states that tawhid is the most excellent act of worship. The hadith from Abu Sa‘id reinforces that even prayer is secondary.
The phrase 'ashraf al-‘ibādāt' is key.
3
Reflect on why tawhid is most excellent
Consider that tawhid is the foundation; all other acts depend on it. The hadith implies that if anything were better, Allah would have commanded it to the angels.
4
Examine the ten principles
The author notes that the previous definition is one of the ten principles. The verse lists three: definition, subject matter, and fruit. Understand that every science has these foundations.
This shows the poem's methodological awareness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : قد جاء بغير التوحيد، فلِمَ اقتصر الناظم على التوحيد؟ أجيب بأنه خصَّهُ لأنه أشرفُ العبادات، ويليه الصلاة; كما في حديث أبي سعيد: إن الله تعالى لم يفرض شيئاً أفضل من التوحيد والصلاة، ولو كان شيء أفضل منه، لافترضَهُ على ملائكته ; منهم رائع ومنهم ساجد (۷) . والحد السابق هو أحد المبادئ العشرة المنظومة في قول بعضهم (۸) : [من الرجز] إِنَّ مَبَادِي كُلِّ فَنْ عَشَرَهُ الحَدُّ وَالمَوْضُوعُ ثُمَّ الثَّمَرَهُ
If it is said: the poem has come with other than tawhīd, so why did the poet limit it to tawhīd? It is answered: He specified it because it is the noblest of acts of worship, and after it comes prayer, as in the hadith of Abu Sa‘id: Verily Allah did not prescribe anything better than tawhīd and prayer; and if there were anything better than it, He would have prescribed it to His angels; among them are bowing and prostrating. And the previous definition is one of the ten principles expressed in the words of some: [ar-Rajaz] Indeed the principles of every art are ten: the definition, the subject matter, then the fruit.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer. The objector notes that the poem deals with other topics besides tawhid (e.g., prophethood, etc.), so why does the poet (Ibn al-Jawharī?) title it 'Jawharat al-Tawhid' or limit the opening to tawhid? The answer is that tawhid is the most excellent form of worship, even surpassing prayer, as shown by a hadith: Allah did not prescribe anything better than tawhid and prayer, and if there were anything better, He would have prescribed it to the angels (who are constantly worshipping). The poet specifies tawhid for this reason. Then the author notes that the previous definition (whichever definition he referred to earlier, likely the definition of the science or of tawhid) is one of the ten principles (mabādi‘) that every art has. He quotes a verse listing three of those principles: definition (ḥadd), subject matter (mawḍū‘), and fruit (thamara). The ten principles are a standard classification in Islamic sciences.

Key Terms
أشرف العبادات
حديث أبي سعيد
المبادئ العشرة
الحد
الموضوع
الثمرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Limiting the poem to tawhid means the poet thinks only tawhid matters and other beliefs are unimportant.
Correction: The poet specifies tawhid because it is the most excellent act of worship, but the poem covers other topics, and the science of tawhid is organized under the ten principles.

Checkpoint
Why did the poet limit his poem's opening to tawhid according to the answer?
A) Because tawhid is the only obligation
B) Because it is the noblest of acts of worship
C) Because the angels only do tawhid
D) Because other topics are not important

Key Takeaway
The poet's exclusive focus on tawhid in the title is due to its supreme merit, and the poem is methodologically grounded in the ten principles of Islamic sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that tawhid is the noblest act of worship, how would your daily priorities change? What is one specific action you will take today to place tawhid above all else?
?
The poet limited his poem to tawhid because of its supreme excellence. In your own life, does tawhid hold that central position, or do other concerns often take precedence? Reflect on a moment this week where you let something else overshadow your awareness of Allah.
?
The passage emphasizes that Islamic knowledge is methodically structured (the ten principles). Are you systematic in your pursuit of religious knowledge, or do you approach it haphazardly? Commit to one concrete step to bring more order to your learning today.

The Ten Principles and the Subject of Kalam
The author lists the ten principles and defines the subject of kalam in four parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ten universal principles of any science and how ilm al-kalam defines its subject matter as God's essence, the prophets, the contingent world, and scriptural beliefs.

Core Idea
Every science begins with ten principles; kalam's subject is the highest possible.

This lesson introduces the foundational framework of Islamic sciences: the ten principles (mabadi) that define any discipline. The author first presents a poem summarizing these principles, then applies them to ilm al-kalam by defining its subject. Understanding this is key to grasping why kalam is considered the most noble of religious sciences.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem on principles
The passage opens with a poetic list of the ten principles of any art. Read it carefully and note which principles are mentioned.
The poem serves as a memory aid for students of Islamic sciences.
2
Identify which principle is being explained first
After the poem, the author begins applying the principles to kalam, starting with its definition (already given) and then its subject. Focus on how the subject is broken into four parts.
3
Break down the subject of kalam
The subject includes: (1) God's essence in terms of necessary, impossible, and possible; (2) the same for messengers; (3) contingent beings as proofs for the Creator; (4) transmitted matters (sam'iyyat) as beliefs. Write these down.
Each component addresses a different aspect of kalam's scope.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why the subject of kalam is defined so broadly. How does this reflect the science's aim to defend tawhid with certainty?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحد السابق هو أحد المبادئ العشرة المنظومة في قول بعضهم (۸) : [من الرجز] إِنَّ مَبَادِي كُلِّ فَنْ عَشَرَهُ الحَدُّ وَالمَوْضُوعُ ثُمَّ الثَّمَرَهُ وَفَضْلُهُ وَنِسْبَةٌ وَالوَاضِعْ وَالاسْمُ الاسْتِمْدَادُ حُكْمُ الشَّارِعُ مَسَائِلٌ، وَالبَعْضُ بِالبَعْضِ اكْتَفَى وَمَنْ دَرَى الجَمِيعَ حَازَ الشَّرَفا فحد هذا الفنّ لغة وشرعاً ما تقدّم. وموضوعُهُ : ذاتُ اللهِ من حيث ما يجب له وما يستحيل وما يجوز، وذاتُ الرُّسل كذلك، والممكنُ من حيث إنه يُتوصل به إلى وجود صانعه (1)، والسمعيات من حيث اعتقادها. وثمرته : معرفةُ الله بالبراهين القطعية، والفوز بالسعادة الأبدية. وفضله : أنه أشرفُ العلوم ؛ لكونه متعلقاً بذاته تعالى وذاتِ رسله وما يتبع ذلك، والمتعلّق - بكسر اللام - يشرف بشرف المتعلَّق - بفتحها - (٢) . ونسبته : أنه أصلُ العلوم الدينيَّة (۳) وما سواه فرغ وما سواه فرح عنه، وما أحسن قول القائل : [من الخفيف] أَيُّهَا المُقْتَدِي (٤) لِتَطْلُبَ عِلْماً كُلُّ عِلْمٍ عَبْدٌ لِعِلْمِ الكَلَامِ (۵) تَطْلُبُ الفِقْهَ كَي تُصَحِّحَ حُكْماً ثُمَّ أَغْفَلْتَ مُنْزِلَ الأَحْكَامِ وواضعه أبو الحسن الأشعري ومَنْ تبعَهُ، وأبو منصور الماتريدي ومَنْ تبعه؛ بمعنى: أنهم دوّنوا كتبه وردُّوا الشُّبة التي أوردتها المعتزلة (٦)، وإلا فالتوحيد جاءَ به كل نبي من لدن آدم إلى يوم القيامة.
And the previous definition is one of the ten principles versified in the saying of some: [in rajaz] Indeed the principles of every art are ten: the definition, the subject, then the fruit, its virtue, its relation, the founder, the name, the derivation, the ruling of the legislator, [and] the issues. And some suffice with some of them, and whoever knows all of them has attained honor. So the definition of this art, linguistically and legally, is what preceded. Its subject is: the essence of God in terms of what is necessary for Him, what is impossible for Him, and what is permissible for Him; and similarly the essence of the messengers; and the contingent being insofar as it leads to the existence of its creator; and the transmitted matters (sam'iyyat) in terms of believing in them. Its fruit is: knowledge of God by definitive proofs and attainment of eternal happiness. Its virtue is that it is the noblest of sciences because it is related (muta'alliq) to His essence, may He be exalted, and the essence of His messengers and what follows that; and the related thing (al-muta'alliq) is honored by the honor of that to which it is related (al-muta'allaq). Its relation is that it is the root of the religious sciences, and everything else is a branch thereof, and it is independent of them. And how excellent is the saying of the poet: [in khafif] O you who seek knowledge, every science is a servant to the science of kalam; you seek fiqh to correct a ruling, then you ignore the Revealer of rulings. Its founder is Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and those who followed him, and Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and those who followed him; meaning that they compiled its books and refuted the doubts presented by the Mu'tazila; otherwise, tawhid was brought by every prophet from Adam until the Day of Resurrection.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage explains the ten principles (mabadi) of any science and applies them to ilm al-kalam. The author first quotes a poem listing the ten principles: definition, subject, fruit, virtue, relation, founder, name, derivation, ruling of the legislator, and issues. Then he applies each to kalam: (1) the definition is the legal definition of tawhid given earlier. (2) The subject includes God's essence (with its necessary, impossible, and contingent aspects), similarly the prophets, the possible leading to the Creator, and scriptural matters (sam'iyyat). (3) The fruit is certain knowledge of God and eternal bliss. (4) The virtue is that it is the noblest science because its subject is the noblest (God and His messengers). (5) The relation is that kalam is the root of all religious sciences; all others are branches dependent on it. He quotes a couplet reinforcing that kalam is the master science. (6) The founder: Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and their followers who codified the science and refuted the Mu'tazila, though the essence of tawhid was taught by all prophets from Adam onward.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study Islamic theology, do you treat it as a complete science with clear principles, or do you pick and choose topics casually? How does recognizing kalam's ten principles change your approach to learning it?
?
The subject of kalam includes God, prophets, the universe, and revelation. Which of these areas do you neglect most in your personal reflection? What would it cost you to give it the attention it deserves?
?
If the fruit of kalam is certainty about the highest realities, what fruit are you actually producing in your daily life? Does your knowledge of God's attributes make you more worshipful, or is it merely academic?

The Fruit, Virtue, and Relation of Kalam
The author describes the fruit, virtue, and relation of kalam, including a poetic tribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why ilm al-kalam is considered the noblest science and how it serves as the root of all religious disciplines.

Core Idea
Kalam gives certainty about God and is the master of all religious sciences.

After defining kalam's subject, the author explains its fruit (the goal), its virtue (why it is superior), and its relation to other sciences. The passage includes a powerful poetic statement that every other science is a servant to kalam. This lesson helps you appreciate the central role of kalam in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fruit of kalam
Read the sentence starting with 'Its fruit is'. Note the two outcomes: knowledge of God through definitive proofs, and attainment of eternal happiness.
The fruit combines intellectual certainty and spiritual reward.
2
Understand why kalam is the noblest science
The virtue is based on the honor of its subject. Since kalam deals with God and His prophets, it is inherently noble. Reflect on the principle that a discipline's value derives from its object.
3
Examine kalam's relation to other sciences
The passage states kalam is the root (asl) and others are branches (far'). The couplet reinforces this by calling all other sciences servants of kalam. Think about how fiqh, tafsir, etc. depend on kalam's proofs.
4
Reflect on the quote
The poet criticizes those who study fiqh without acknowledging the Revealer of rulings. This drives home that kalam is foundational for all religious knowledge.

The Founders of Kalam and the Universality of Tawhid
The author names the founders of kalam and clarifies they are codifiers, not innovators of tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the role of Imam al-Ash'ari and Imam al-Maturidi as codifiers of kalam, and that tawhid was taught by all prophets from Adam onward.

Core Idea
Ash'ari and Maturidi compiled kalam, but tawhid is as old as prophethood itself.

The final principle applied to kalam is its founder (wadi'). The passage names the two great Sunni schools—Ash'ari and Maturidi—and clarifies that they are not originators of the message of tawhid, but rather systematizers who defended it against innovations. This lesson resolves a common misconception and highlights the continuity of Islamic prophetic teaching.

Guided Path
1
Identify the founders named
Read the sentence beginning with 'Its founder is...'. Note two names: Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, along with their followers.
These represent the two main Sunni theological schools.
2
Understand their precise role
The author adds 'meaning that they compiled its books and refuted the doubts of the Mu'tazila.' This clarifies that they did not invent kalam but organized it and defended orthodox belief.
3
Grasp the important distinction
The exception phrase 'otherwise, tawhid was brought by every prophet from Adam until the Day of Resurrection' emphasizes that the core message of monotheism is universal and predates Ash'ari and Maturidi.
4
Reflect on the significance
Why is this clarification necessary? How does it protect the status of earlier prophets and the continuity of revealed truth?
