The Origins and Variants of 'Ammā Ba‘d'
The commentator explains the structure and variants of the introductory phrase 'ammā ba‘d'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic origins and variants of the Arabic introductory phrase 'ammā ba‘d' and the grammatical reasoning behind its structure.

Core Idea
The phrase 'ammā ba‘d' is a condensed conditional structure, with variants like 'wa ba‘d'.

This lesson unpacks the seemingly simple phrase 'ammā ba‘d' (أما بعد), showing how it evolved from a full conditional clause. We'll see how scholars debate its grammatical components and trace its use back to Prophet David.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the technical grammatical terms used.
2
Identify the original conditional structure
The passage traces 'ammā ba‘d' back to 'mahmā yakun min shay'. Find this transformation and understand how 'ammā' replaced the conditional elements.
The substitution of 'ammā' for the entire conditional clause is a key linguistic point.
3
Explore the variant and historical origin
Note that some say 'wa ba‘d' instead of 'ammā ba‘d'. Then read about the dispute over who first used the phrase and its association with David and 'faṣl al-khiṭāb'.
The historical claim is based on a hadith report.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة ويكن فعل الشرط، وهو مضارعُ (كانَ) التامَّةِ ، وفاعله ضمير مستتر تقديره هو يعود على (مهما) و (من شيء) بيان لمهما وإن كان شأن البيان التخصيص ؛ فقد يكونُ مساوياً إشارة إلى أن المراد الجنس بتمامه ، فحذفت (مهما) و(يكن) و(من شيء)، وأقيمت (أما ) مقام ذلك، ثم إن بعضهم ينطق بذلك ويقول: (أما بعد) كما وبعضهم يحذف (أما) ويأتي بدلها بالواو فيقول : ( وبعد) كما هنا ؛ فالواو نائبةُ النائب (۱) هو السنة. وهل الظرف من معمولات الشرط أو من معمولات الجزاء؟ خلافٌ، والراجح كونه من معمولات الجزاء؛ ليكون المعلق عليه مطلقاً (۲)، وهو أبلغ في التحقيق؛ لأن المعنى عليه : إن وُجد شيءٌ في الدنيا مطلقاً، فأقول بعد ... إلى آخره، ولا يرد أن الفاءَ لا يعملُ ما بعدها فيما قبلها؛ لتوسعهم في الظروف. و (بعد) ظرف زمان كثيراً، ومكان قليلاً (۳) ، وهي هنا صالحة للزمان باعتبار النطق، وللمكان باعتبار الرَّقْم . واختلف فى أول مَنْ نطق بها على أقوال: (٤) أقربها : أنه داود، وكانت له فصل الخطاب (4) ؛ أي: يفصلُ بها بين الحقِّ والباطل. وقيل : يُفصلُ بها بين نوع من الكلام ونوع آخر منه (۱) وقد أنشدوا فى (وبعد) وكون الواو نائبة النائب : وما وأولها شرطٌ يَليهِ جوابٌ قَرَتُهُ بالفاءِ حَتمَا؟ هي الواو التي قُرنَتْ بَبَعد وأمَّا أصلها والأصل مهما (۲) وقوله : (مطلقاً) أي: عن التقييد يكونه بعد البسملة وما بعدها، وهذا الإطلاق باعتبار ظاهر اللفظ، وأما باعتبار الواقع فالمعلق عليه - وهو وجود شيء في الدنيا - مقيد بكونه بعد البسملة وما بعدها ؛ لأن الفرض أنه أتى من أول الأمر بالبسملة وما بعدها، والمضارع الواقع بعد (مهما ) التي هي الأصل للاستقبال كما هو شأن الأفعال الواقعة بعد أدوات التعليق، وحينئذ فقوله : وبعد معناه: مهما يكن من شيء في المستقبل، فتعين أن يكون وجود الشيء مُقيداً بكونه بعد البسملة، وما بعدها باعتبار الواقع . انتهى أجهوري. (۳) كقولك : داري بعد دارك . (٤) أخرجه ابن أبي شيبة في مصنفه» (٢٥٧/٥). قوله : (فَالعِلْمُ ...) إلى آخره ؛ أي : فأقول لك : العلم ... إلى آخره؛ لأنَّ كونَ العلم بأصل الدين محتماً أمرٌ متحقق في نفسه، وُجد شيءٌ في الدنيا أو لا، فلا يصح جعله جواب الشرط، فلا بد من تقدير القول. فإن قلت : إذا حذف القول وجب حذف الفاء معه كما نصَّ عليه الأشموني. قلت : المسألة خلافية؛ لأن هناك قولاً بجواز ذكر الفاء مع حذف القول كما ذكره السيوطي في (همع الهوامع (۱) ، والفاء واقعة في جواب (أما) المقدرة، أو في جواب الواو النائبة عنها .
The introduction: 'and it is the verb of the condition, which is the imperfect of the complete 'kāna', and its subject is an implicit pronoun referring back to 'mahmā' and 'min shay' as a specification for 'mahmā', although the norm of specification is restriction; it may be equal, indicating that what is intended is the entire genus. So 'mahmā', 'yakun', and 'min shay' were omitted, and 'ammā' was put in their place. Then some people utter it and say 'ammā ba‘d', while others omit 'ammā' and substitute it with the wāw, saying 'wa ba‘d' as here; so the wāw is the substitute of the substitute (1) he is the sunna. And is the adverb from the operative parts of the condition or from the operative parts of the result? There is a dispute, and the preponderant view is that it is from the operative parts of the result, so that the thing upon which the [statement] is suspended is absolute (2), and it is more emphatic in verification; because the meaning is: if something absolutely exists in the world, then I say 'after...' to the end. And it is not countered that the fā' does not operate what is after it on what is before it, due to their extension in adverbs. And 'ba‘d' is an adverb of time frequently, and of place rarely (3), and it is here suitable for time according to utterance, and for place according to writing. And there is disagreement concerning the first person who uttered it, with sayings (4): the closest of them: that it was David, and he had the decisive speech (faṣl al-khiṭāb); i.e., he used it to distinguish between truth and falsehood. And it is said: one uses it to distinguish between one type of speech and another type. (1) And they have recited concerning 'wa ba‘d' and that the wāw is the substitute of the substitute: 'What thing whose first is a condition, its answer is necessarily followed by the fā'? It is the wāw that is joined with 'ba‘d', and its origin is 'ammā', and the origin is 'mahmā'. (2) And his saying: 'absolute' means unrestricted by being after the basmala and what follows; this absoluteness is according to the apparent wording, but according to reality, the thing upon which it is suspended—which is the existence of something in the world—is restricted by being after the basmala and what follows; because it is assumed that he begins from the first with the basmala and what follows, and the imperfect verb after 'mahmā', which is the original particle, is for the future as is the case with verbs after tools of suspension. Then his saying 'wa ba‘d' means: 'Whatever thing occurs in the future', so it is determined that the existence of the thing is restricted by being after the basmala and what follows according to reality. End of Ajhurī. (3) Like your saying: 'My house is after your house.' (4) Ibn Abī Shayba narrated it in his 'Musannaf' (5/257). His saying: '(fa-al-‘ilmu...)' to the end means: I say to you: knowledge... to the end; because the fact that knowledge of the fundamentals of religion is obligatory is a matter verified in itself, regardless of whether something exists in the world or not, so it is not valid to make it the answer to the condition. Thus it is necessary to assume an implied saying (al-qawl). If you say: If the saying (al-qawl) is omitted, the fā' must also be omitted, as al-Ashmūnī stated. I say: the issue is a matter of dispute; because there is a saying permitting the mention of the fā' with the omission of the saying, as al-Suyūṭī mentioned in 'Ham‘ al-Hawāmi‘' (1), and the fā' occurs in the answer of the implied 'ammā', or in the answer of the wāw that substitutes for it.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a detailed linguistic and grammatical analysis of the introductory phrase 'ammā ba‘d' (أما بعد) used in Arabic works. The commentator begins by tracing the phrase's origin to a conditional structure: originally, the phrase was 'mahmā yakun min shay' (مهما يكن من شيء, 'whatever thing there is'), which was abbreviated by dropping 'mahmā', 'yakun', and 'min shay', and replacing them with 'ammā'. He notes that some people say 'ammā ba‘d' while others say 'wa ba‘d', with the wāw acting as a substitute for 'ammā'. He then discusses whether the adverb 'ba‘d' belongs grammatically to the condition or to the answer (the result clause), concluding that the preponderant view is that it belongs to the answer, to make the statement's suspension absolute. He also cites the origin of this phrase as being used by Prophet David (Dawūd, peace be upon him) for 'faṣl al-khiṭāb' (decisive speech) to distinguish truth from falsehood. Finally, the commentator addresses an objection: if the implied 'qawl' (saying) is omitted when supplying the answer to the condition, the 'fā'' (the particle marking the answer) should also be omitted. He responds that it is permissible to keep the 'fā'' based on other scholarly opinions, specifically citing al-Suyūṭī.

Correction: In fact, 'wa ba‘d' is a variant where the wāw substitutes for 'ammā', which itself replaces a full conditional clause.

D) 'law kāna shay'

Key Takeaway

Resolving a Grammatical Objection on 'Fa'
The commentator addresses a grammatical objection regarding the conditional particle fa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a grammatical objection regarding the conditional particle 'fa' is resolved by appealing to scholarly disagreement and interpretive alternatives.

Core Idea
Grammar in kalam is flexible: meaning can override strict rules.

This passage addresses a subtle grammatical issue arising from the line 'fa al-'ilm...' in the poem. The objection is that if knowledge is always obligatory, it cannot be the answer to a conditional. The commentator argues that the answer is an implied verb of speech, and the retention of 'fa' is justified by alternative interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic carefully, then the translation. Notice the structure: objection and answer.
2
Identify the problem
The issue is whether 'fa' can remain when the verb of speech is omitted. Understand why the commentator thinks a verb must be assumed.
The conditional structure requires an answer that is conditioned.
3
Examine the objection and answer
The objector cites al-Ashmuni's rule. The answer cites al-Suyuti's contrary position and provides two reconciliations: implied 'amma' or substituting 'waw'.
4
Reflect on the significance
This shows that even in strict disciplines like grammar, there is flexibility, especially to preserve correct meaning in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فَالعِلْمُ ...) إلى آخره ؛ أي : فأقول لك : العلم ... إلى آخره؛ لأنَّ كونَ العلم بأصل الدين محتماً أمرٌ متحقق في نفسه، وُجد شيءٌ في الدنيا أو لا، فلا يصح جعله جواب الشرط، فلا بد من تقدير القول. فإن قلت : إذا حذف القول وجب حذف الفاء معه كما نصَّ عليه الأشموني. قلت : المسألة خلافية؛ لأن هناك قولاً بجواز ذكر الفاء مع حذف القول كما ذكره السيوطي في (همع الهوامع (۱) ، والفاء واقعة في جواب (أما) المقدرة، أو في جواب الواو النائبة عنها . (1).
His statement: '(So knowledge...)' to its end; i.e., I say to you: knowledge ... to its end; because the fact that knowledge of the foundation of religion is obligatory is a matter established in itself, whether something exists in the world or not, so it is not valid to make it the answer of the conditional, so it is necessary to assume the verb 'say'. If you say: If the verb 'say' is deleted, it is necessary to delete the fa with it, as al-Ashmuni stated. I say: The issue is disputed; because there is a position permitting the mention of fa along with the deletion of the verb 'say' as al-Suyuti mentioned in (Ham' al-Hawami' (1)), and the fa occurs in the answer of the implied (amma), or in the answer of the waw that substitutes for it.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage contains a grammatical objection and answer within the commentary on the line 'fa al-'ilm...' from the poem. The commentator explains that the word 'al-'ilm' (knowledge) cannot be the direct answer (jawab al-shart) of the conditional because the obligation of knowledge is a constant truth, independent of any condition. Therefore, the verb 'al-qaul' (I say) must be assumed as the actual answer. An objection is then raised: if the verb 'al-qaul' is deleted, the conditional particle 'fa' should also be deleted, as grammarians like al-Ashmuni rule. The answer is that this is a disputed matter; some scholars, like al-Suyuti, allow keeping the 'fa' even when the verb of speech is omitted. The commentator then reconciles this by stating that the 'fa' can be interpreted as the answer of an implied 'amma' (as for) or as the answer of the 'waw' that substitutes for 'amma'. This shows that kalam texts sometimes employ grammatical flexibility for the sake of meaning.

Key Terms
القول
الفاء
جواب الشرط
أما المقدرة
الواو النائبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Grammatical rules are absolute and cannot be violated in sacred texts.
Correction: This passage shows that even in theological commentary, scholars allow grammatical flexibility based on context and meaning. The retention of 'fa' is justified through alternative interpretations and acknowledged disagreement.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the grammatical justification for keeping the 'fa' when the verb of speech is omitted?
A) It is in the answer of an implied 'amma' or the substituting waw.
B) The 'fa' is always retained regardless of deletion.
C) The verb of speech cannot be omitted in any case.
D) The conditional sentence is defective.

Key Takeaway
In kalam, grammatical rules may be interpreted flexibly to preserve theological meaning.

The Meaning of 'Asl al-Din' and Its Individual Obligation
Explanation of the term 'asl al-din' and the ruling that knowing the foundations with summary evidence is an individual obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and technical meaning of 'asl al-din' and why knowing the foundations of religion with at least summary evidence is an individual duty for every Muslim.

Core Idea
The foundations of religion are the principles upon which all rulings rest; knowing them is fard 'ayn.

Sanusi begins his poem with the phrase 'bi-asl al-din', the foundation of religion. The commentator explains that this singular term actually encompasses all the principles (usul) of religion. He also clarifies why this science is called 'usul al-din' and then establishes a crucial ruling: every Muslim, male or female, must know every creedal point with at least summary evidence. This is an individual obligation based on the Quranic command 'Know that there is no god but Allah'. Let's explore the meaning and obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the explanation of 'asl al-din'
Read the passage where the commentator explains that 'asl' is singular but implies all principles due to poetic necessity.
Notice the discussion about the title 'usul al-din' and the part-to-whole addition.
2
Understand why the science is called 'usul al-din'
The commentator notes that the title indicates praise because the religion is built upon these foundations.
3
Grasp the individual obligation
Know that every legally responsible person must know each creed with summary evidence, citing Quran 47:19.
This is fard 'ayn, not optional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : بأَصْلِ الدِّينِ ) أي : بأصولِهِ (۱) وقواعده، فهو مفرد مضاف يعم، وأفرد الأصل مع أن هذا الفنّ ملقب بأصول الدين، لضرورة النظم، فهو من التصرُّف في العلم لما ذكر . (۲) وقيل : إنه ليس إشارة للمعنى العلمي، والإضافة في أصول الدين من إضافة الجزء للكل؛ لأن الدين هو الأحكام أصلية كانت أو فرعية، وهذا اللقب يشعر بمدح هذا الفنّ؛ لابتناء الدين عليه، ولمَّا لاحظ المصنف في العلم معنى الجزم، عداه بالباء قوله : (مُحَتَّمٌ) أي: حتَّمه الشارعُ وأوجبه ، ولم يرخُص في تركه؛ لقوله تعالى : فاعلم أنَّهُ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا اللهُ [محمد : ۱۹]، فيجب على كل مكلَّف من ذكر وأنثى وجوباً عينيا معرفةُ كلِّ عقيدة بدليل ولو إجماليًّا، وأما معرفتها بالدليل التفصيلي، ففرضُ كفاية ) ، فيجب على أهل كلِّ قطر أي ناحية يشقُ الوصول منها إلى غيرها أن يكونَ فيهم من يعرفها بالدليل التفصيلي ؛ لأنه ربما طرأت شبهة فيدفعها، وبعضهم أوجبَ (۳) قال إمام الحرمين عبد الملك الجويني رحمه الله تعالى في كتابه العظيم نهاية المطلب» (٤١٧/١٧) : (ولو قيل : العلم المترجم بالكلام هل يستلحق بفرائض الكفايات؟ قلنا : لو بقى الناسُ على ما كانوا عليه في صفوة الإسلام لكنا نقول : لا يجب التشاغل بالكلام، وقد كنا ننتهي إلى النهي عن [؟] به والآن قد ثارت الآراء، واضطربت الأهواء، ولا سبيل إلى ترك البدع، فلا ينتظم الإعراض عن الناس يتهالكون على الردى، فحق على طلبة العلم أن يُعدُّوا عتاد الدعوة إلى المسلك الحق، والذريعة التامة إلى حَلَّ الشبه، إلى أن قال رضي الله عنه : ( فإذن ؛ علم التوحيد من أهم ما يُطلب في زماننا هذا ، وإن استمكن الإنسان من رد الخلق إلى ما كانُوا عليه أولاً .. فهو المطلوب، وهيهات! فهو أبعد من رجوع اللبن إلى الضرع في مستقر العادة). وقال إمامنا حجة الإسلام أبو حامد الغزالي رحمه الله تعالى في الوجيز» كما في «شرحه» (۳۶۸/۱۱) : ( والقيام بدفع شبهة المبتدعة فرض كفاية).
His statement: 'with the foundation of the religion' i.e., with its principles and rules, for it is a singular possessive noun that indicates generality, and he used the singular 'foundation' even though this art is called 'the principles of the religion', due to poetic necessity, so it is a manipulation in the name for what was mentioned. And it is said: It is not a reference to the scientific meaning, and the addition in 'principles of the religion' is an addition of part to whole, because the religion consists of rulings, whether original or subsidiary, and this title indicates praise of this art because the religion is built upon it. And since the author in 'knowledge' considered the meaning of certainty, he made it transitive with the letter 'ba'. His statement: 'obligatory' i.e., the Legislator made it obligatory and required it, and did not permit its abandonment, due to His statement: 'Know that there is no god but Allah' [Muhammad 19]. So it is obligatory upon every legally responsible person, male or female, as an individual obligation to know every creed by evidence, even if summary. As for knowing it by detailed evidence, it is a collective obligation. So it is obligatory upon the people of every region, i.e., any area from which access to others is difficult, that there be among them those who know it by detailed evidence, because perhaps a doubt arises and he would repel it. And some made it obligatory... Imam al-Haramayn 'Abd al-Malik al-Juwayni said in his great book Nihayat al-Matlab: (If it is said: Is the science translated as kalam included among the collective obligations? We say: If people remained as they were in the best of Islam, we would say that occupying oneself with kalam is not obligatory, and we would end at the prohibition of [?] it. But now opinions have arisen, desires have become confused, and there is no way to abandon innovation, so it is not appropriate to turn away from people who are rushing to destruction. So it is incumbent upon seekers of knowledge to prepare the weapon of calling to the true path and the perfect means to resolve doubts. ... so then; the science of tawhid is one of the most important things to be sought in our time, and if one is able to return people to what they were upon initially, that is what is sought. But alas! That is more distant than the return of milk to the udder in the natural order.) And our Imam, the Proof of Islam, Abu Hamid al-Ghazali said in al-Wajiz, as in its commentary: (The undertaking to repel the doubts of innovators is a collective obligation.)
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage explains the phrase 'bi-asl al-din' from the poem. The commentator clarifies that 'asl' (foundation) is singular but implies the plural 'usul' (principles) due to poetic necessity, and that the science is called 'usul al-din' (foundations of religion) because it deals with the fundamental principles. The addition is part-to-whole, as the religion includes both original and subsidiary rulings. The title 'usul al-din' honors this art because the religion is built on it. The commentator then discusses the obligation (muhattam) of knowing these foundations, citing Quran 47:19. This knowledge is an individual obligation (fard 'ayn) for every legally responsible person, even with summary evidence. However, knowing it with detailed evidence is a collective obligation (fard kifaya) on the people of each region, as they need someone to repel doubts. The passage ends with two authoritative quotes: from Imam al-Juwayni arguing that in times of widespread innovation, studying kalam becomes a collective duty, and from al-Ghazali stating that repelling the doubts of innovators is a collective obligation. This underscores the practical necessity of kalam in defending the faith.

Key Terms
أصل الدين
فرض عين
الدليل الإجمالي
محتّم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that knowing the foundations of religion is only required for scholars or men.
Correction: The text says it is obligatory upon every legally responsible person, male and female, with at least summary evidence.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, the phrase 'bi-asl al-din' in the poem means:
A) With the single foundation of religion
B) With the principles and rules of the religion
C) With the detailed evidence
D) With the collective obligation

Key Takeaway
Knowing the foundations of religion with at least summary evidence is an individual obligation on every Muslim, based on the Quranic command 'Know that there is no god but Allah'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that knowing every creed with at least summary evidence is an individual obligation on you. Which of your religious beliefs can you currently defend with a clear verse or hadith? Identify one that you only accept from family or culture. What will you do today to find its evidence?
?
The commentator says the title 'usul al-din' praises this art because religion is built on it. On what foundation is your faith actually built? Is it on the Quran and Sunnah, or on what people around you say? If it were tested, would it stand? Write down a concrete step to build it on solid proof this week.
?
If you were asked tomorrow to give a summary proof for the existence of Allah, could you? This lesson makes it a personal duty. Do you feel the weight of that? If not, why? And if you do, commit now to memorize the evidence from Quran 47:19 and the argument from contingency. Will you do it?

Detailed Knowledge as a Collective Obligation and the Necessity of Kalam
Explanation that knowing creed with detailed evidence is a collective obligation, with supporting quotes from al-Juwayni and al-Ghazali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between individual and collective obligations in creedal knowledge, and why the detailed study of kalam becomes a communal duty in times of innovation.

Core Idea
Defending the faith requires specialists in detailed evidence; it is a collective obligation upon each region.

In the previous lesson, we learned that knowing creed with summary evidence is an individual duty. But what about deep, detailed knowledge? The commentator now states that this is a collective obligation (fard kifaya) on the people of each region. He then supports this with powerful statements from two great scholars: Imam al-Juwayni and Imam al-Ghazali. These quotes show that in times of widespread innovation, studying kalam becomes essential for protecting the community. Let's examine this ruling and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on detailed evidence as fard kifaya
Read the passage where the commentator states that knowing creed with detailed evidence is a collective obligation.
Notice the phrase 'fard kifaya' and its application.
2
Understand the reason: to repel doubts
The commentator gives the reason: 'because perhaps a doubt arises and he would repel it'.
3
Read al-Juwayni's quote
Read al-Juwayni's argument from Nihayat al-Matlab about the necessity of kalam in times of innovation.
Note that he contrasts the early Islamic period with the present.
4
Read al-Ghazali's statement
Read al-Ghazali's succinct statement from al-Wajiz that repelling doubts is a collective obligation.
This reinforces the previous point.

Source Text

Detailed knowledge of creed is a collective obligation on each region, and in times of innovation, studying kalam becomes essential for defending the faith.

The Reason Behind Writing the Poem on Usul al-Din
Explains the author's purpose behind writing the poem

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's purpose in composing this poem and the meaning of 'needs clarification' in the context of the science of usul al-din.

Core Idea
The science of usul al-din itself required clarification, prompting the poem.

Before diving into the theological content, the commentator clarifies the author's own motivation. The opening line 'needs clarification' is not about the reader's understanding but about the discipline's need for systematic explanation. This lesson unpacks that statement and sets the stage for the entire poem.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the direct meaning.
Notice the commentary structure: it explains the author's statement word by word.
2
Identify the pronoun reference
The passage clarifies that the pronoun in 'يحتاج' refers to the science (العلم) as a codified discipline, not to a person or the act of understanding. This is a key point.
This prevents a common misunderstanding that the need is personal rather than disciplinary.
3
Understand the purpose
The author's purpose is to explain why he wrote the poem: to clarify the science of usul al-din. The term 'التبيين' means explanation, highlighting the need for systematic exposition.
This frames the entire poem as a response to a real intellectual need.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بَحْتَاجُ لِلتَّبْبِينِ) غرضُهُ بذلك بيان السبب الحامل له على وضع هذه المنظومة في أصول الدين دون غيره من العلوم والضمير في (يحتاج) للعلم لا بمعنى الإدراك، بل بمعنى الفنِّ المدون، ويصح أن يكون الضمير عائداً لأصل الدين؛ أي: للفنّ الملقب بأصول الدين. و(التبيين): التوضيح وإنما احتاج هذا الفن للتبيين.
His saying: 'needs clarification' – his purpose by that is to explain the reason that motivated him to compose this poem in the foundations of religion (usul al-din) rather than other sciences. The pronoun in 'needs' refers to the science, not in the sense of perception, but in the sense of the codified discipline. And it is valid that the pronoun refers back to 'the foundation of religion', i.e., to the discipline called usul al-din. And 'clarification' (al-tabyeen) means explanation. And this discipline needed clarification.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage explains the author's statement that this science 'needs clarification.' It identifies the purpose of the poem: to explain the reason for writing it about usul al-din. It clarifies that 'needs' refers to the science as a codified discipline, not as mere understanding. It also defines 'tabyeen' as explanation, and reiterates that the discipline required clarification.

Key Terms
تبيين
أصول الدين
فن مدون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The pronoun in 'يحتاج' refers to a person's individual knowledge or understanding.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the pronoun refers to the science (العلم) as a codified discipline, not to a person or the act of perception.

Checkpoint
What does the author mean by 'يحتاج للتبيين'?
A) The person needs to clarify his beliefs.
B) The science of usul al-din needs explanation.
C) The poem needs to be clarified.
D) The author needs clarification.

Key Takeaway
The science of usul al-din needed clarification, which motivated the author to compose this poem.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read or listen to Islamic teachings, do you assume you already understand them, or do you recognize that even the discipline itself may need clarification? How has this lesson challenged you to approach learning usul al-din with greater humility?
?
The author saw a need to clarify a science that others might have thought was already clear. Is there an area of your own faith where you have avoided deep clarification because it feels uncomfortable or unnecessary? What is stopping you from seeking that clarification?
?
The pronoun debate shows that precision in language matters in theology. Are you someone who skips over such details, or do you pause to consider the exact meaning? How might your understanding of Islamic theology deepen if you committed to paying attention to such nuances?

Reason for Needing Clarification in Aqidah
The author explains the historical need for clarification in aqidah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical reason why the science of aqidah required clarification and why later scholars included philosophical doubts in their works.

Core Idea
Aqidah needed clarification to refute the doubts of innovators after the 5th century.

This passage from Tuhfat al-Mureed explains why the author composed his poem on usul al-din. It addresses a common criticism: why did later scholars include philosophical arguments in aqidah? The answer lies in the historical emergence of innovators who mixed doubts with philosophy, making it necessary to address them directly.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The author begins by clarifying that the pronoun in 'it needs' refers to the science of aqidah as a codified discipline, not to perception. This sets the stage for the historical explanation.
Pay attention to the two possible references for the pronoun.
2
Identify the cause
The cause for needing clarification is the rise of innovators (mubtadi'a) after the year 500 AH, who brought doubts against earlier established beliefs and mixed them with philosophical principles.
The footnote specifies that 'innovators' here refers to the Mu'tazila.
3
Recognize the method
Later scholars responded by including those very doubts in their works so they could refute them. This was not an innovation but a necessary defensive measure.
The author emphasizes that they only included them for an important purpose.
4
Reflect on the necessity
The inclusion of philosophical arguments was considered obligatory (wajib) to protect the faith, contrary to those who criticized this approach.
Consider how this reasoning applies to later scholarly works in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (يحتاج للتبيين) غرضه بذلك بيان السبب الحامل له على وضع هذه المنظومة في أصول الدين دون غيره من العلوم. والضمير في (يحتاج) للعلم لا بمعنى الإدراك، بل بمعنى الفن المدون، ويصح أن يكون الضمير عائداً لأصل الدين؛ أي: للفن الملقب بأصول الدين. و(التبيين): التوضيح. وإنما احتاج هذا الفن للتبيين لأنه لما حدثت المبتدعة (1) بعد الخمسمائة (2) وكثر جدالهم مع علماء الإسلام، وأوردوا شبهاً على ما قرره الأوائل، وخلطوا تلك الشبه بكثير من القواعد الفلسفية، قصد المتأخرون دفع تلك الشبه، فاحتاجوا إلى إدراجها في كلامهم؛ ليتمكنوا من ردها، فما أدرجوها إلا لغرض مهم؛ بحيث لا يبعد معه الوجوب، خلافاً لمن شنع عليهم في ذلك.

(1) قوله: (لأنه) علة لاحتياجه إلى التبين باعتبار اشتمال التبين على رد الشبه الواردة على الأدلة؛ لأن المراد من التبين هنا ذكر العقائد مع أدلتها، ورد الشبه الواردة على تلك الأدلة، وهذا التعليل منظور فيه إلى رد الشبه فقط. انتهى أجهوري.

(2) قوله: (المبتدعة) المراد من المبتدعة المعتزلة، كما أن المراد بأهل الإسلام أهل السنة، يؤخذ من عبارة الشيخ الأمير في حاشيته على عبد السلام. انتهى أجهوري.
His statement: 'It needs clarification' – his purpose by that is to explain the reason that impelled him to compose this poem on the foundations of religion (usul al-din) to the exclusion of other sciences. The pronoun in 'it needs' refers to the science, not in the sense of perception, but in the sense of the codified art, and it is also valid that the pronoun refers to the foundation of religion, i.e., the art known as usul al-din. And 'clarification' means explanation. This art only needed clarification because when the innovators (mubtadi'a) arose after the five hundredth year, and their arguments with the scholars of Islam increased, and they brought doubts upon what the earlier scholars had established, and mixed those doubts with many philosophical principles, the later scholars aimed to repel those doubts, so they needed to include them in their speech in order to be able to refute them. They only included them for an important purpose, such that obligation is not far-fetched with it, contrary to those who denounced them for that.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage explains why the author composed his poem on usul al-din (foundations of religion) and why the science of aqidah needed clarification. The author first clarifies that the pronoun in 'it needs' refers to the science as a codified discipline, not to perception. 'Clarification' means making things clear. The reason for this need is historical: after the year 500 AH, innovators (specifically the Mu'tazila, as noted in the footnote) emerged and began debating with Sunni scholars, introducing doubts mixed with philosophical principles. Later scholars (mutakallimun) had to include these doubts in their works in order to refute them, which was a necessary duty. The footnotes further explain that this reasoning focuses only on refuting doubts, and that the innovators referred to are the Mu'tazila, while the people of Islam are the Ahl al-Sunnah.

مبتدعة

قواعد فلسفية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Including philosophical arguments in aqidah is an innovation (bid'a) that should be avoided.
Correction: The author argues that later scholars only included philosophical doubts to refute them, which was a necessary duty (wajib) in response to the innovators. It was not an innovation but a defense of orthodoxy.

Checkpoint
Why did later scholars need to include philosophical doubts in their works on aqidah?
A) To impress their audiences with complex arguments.
B) Because the earlier scholars had not addressed those doubts.
C) To refute the doubts raised by innovators after the 5th century.
D) Because it is obligatory to study philosophy.

Key Takeaway
Later scholars included philosophical doubts in aqidah works not as an innovation but as a necessary response to refute the doubts of innovators.

The scholars included philosophical arguments only for the purpose of refutation, not because they accepted them. Resolving the Apparent Contradiction Between Quran 16:78 and the Hadith on Fitrah
The author refutes the claim of contradiction, explaining fitrah correctly.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the apparent contradiction between Quran 16:78 and the hadith on fitrah is resolved, and why fitrah means the state of ignorance rather than innate knowledge of Islam.

Core Idea
Fitrah is not innate knowledge but the state of ignorance when born.

This passage addresses a common theological question: does the hadith 'every child is born upon the fitrah' contradict the Quranic verse 'you knew nothing'? The author refutes this, showing that the apparent conflict is resolved by proper understanding of scriptural precedence and the legal concept of capacity (ahliyyah). This lesson will take you through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation. Notice the author's direct refutation of a mistaken understanding.
The passage contains two main arguments; look for 'First' and 'Then secondly'.
2
Identify the two main arguments
The author first gives precedence to the apparent meaning of the Quran over the hadith. Second, he challenges the use of mafhum al-mukhalafa (concept of opposition) to derive the meaning of the hadith.
Understand that the dispute hinges on how to interpret the hadith's wording.
3
Understand the concept of mafhum al-mukhalafa
Learn why the author argues that mafhum al-mukhalafa does not apply here: because a child's legal status is only by affiliation, and because the concept would limit fitrah to four religions, ignoring others. Also, there is a conflicting narration.
This step helps you see the logical flaws in the opposing view.
4
Grasp the conclusion: fitrah is not Islam
The author concludes that neither the apparent meaning nor the implied meaning of the hadith supports fitrah as Islam. The correct view is that fitrah is the state of ignorance, as stated in the verse.
This is the key takeaway: fitrah = 'you knew nothing'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال تعالى: وَاللَّهُ أَخْرَجَكُم مِّنْ بُطُونِ أُمَّهَيْكُمْ لَا تَعْلَمُونَ شَيْئًا [النحل: 78]، وقد يظن أن هناك تعارضاً مع حديث كل مولود ...، فيفهم من الحديث أن الفطرة هي الإسلام، وليس كذلك لأنه: أولاً: إن ظهر تعارض بين ظاهر القرآن وظاهر السنة نقدم ظاهر القرآن، وظاهر الآية أن الفطرة: لا تعلمون شيئاً، وظاهر الحديث أن الفطرة هي الإسلام. ثم ثانياً: لا تعارض إلا إذا حملنا الحديث على مفهوم المخالفة، ومفهوم المخالفة لم يتم الاستدلال به؛ لأنه يدل على أن أول ما يولد الإنسان فهو مسلم، وكيف هذا؟ والطفل دون البلوغ لا يحكم بإسلامه أو كفره إلا إلحاقاً، فلا تقيم عليه الحد إن ارتد عن الإسلام، ولا نقطع يده إن سرق ما دام دون البلوغ. ولا يتم الاستدلال بمفهوم المخالفة إلا إذا حصرنا المولود بالحالات الأربعة: اليهودية والنصرانية والمجوسية والإسلام، والواقع قد يكون غير ذاك كالشيوعية والرأسمالية مثلاً. ولا يصار إلى مفهوم المخالفة؛ لأنه يوجد رواية لمسلم والبيهقي والحاكم: «فإن كانا مسلمين فمسلم» أي: إن كل مولود إن كان أبواه يهوديين فيهودي، وإن نصرانيين فنصراني، وإن مجوسيين فمجوسي، وإن مسلمين فمسلم. وهذا المفهوم خالف نصاً نبويًّا آخر وهو: «حتى يعرب عنه لسانه، فإن أعرب عنه لسانه فإما شاكراً، وإما كفوراً». فليس يدل ظاهر الحديث ولا منطوقه ولا مفهومه على أن الفطرة هي الإسلام وهي العلم بأمور الدين، بل الصواب أن الفطرة: (لا تعلمون شيئاً) كما دلت عليه الآية.
He said, Most High: 'And Allah brought you out from the wombs of your mothers, you knew nothing' [al-Nahl 16:78]. And one might think that there is a contradiction with the hadith 'every child is born upon the fitrah', and so one understands from the hadith that the fitrah is Islam, but it is not so, because: First: if an apparent contradiction occurs between the apparent meaning of the Quran and the apparent meaning of the Sunnah, we give precedence to the apparent meaning of the Quran, and the apparent meaning of the verse is that the fitrah is 'you knew nothing', while the apparent meaning of the hadith is that the fitrah is Islam. Then secondly: there is no contradiction unless we interpret the hadith according to the concept of opposition (mafhum al-mukhalafa), and the concept of opposition is not used as evidence because it indicates that the first thing that a person is born is a Muslim, and how is that? A child before puberty is not judged as Muslim or disbeliever except by affiliation. So we do not impose the prescribed punishment if he apostatizes from Islam, and we do not cut off his hand if he steals as long as he is pre-pubescent. And the evidence by the concept of opposition is only valid if we restrict the newborn to four states: Judaism, Christianity, Zoroastrianism, and Islam. And reality may be other than that, such as communism and capitalism for example. And one does not resort to the concept of opposition because there exists a narration by Muslim, al-Bayhaqi, and al-Hakim: 'If both parents are Muslims, then the child is a Muslim', meaning: every newborn, if both parents are Jews, then he is a Jew; if Christians, then Christian; if Zoroastrians, then Zoroastrian; if Muslims, then Muslim. And this understanding contradicts another prophetic text: 'until his tongue expresses itself, and if he expresses himself, then either grateful or ungrateful.' So neither the apparent meaning of the hadith, nor its expressed meaning, nor its implied meaning indicates that fitrah is Islam and is knowledge of the matters of religion. Rather, the correct view is that fitrah is 'you knew nothing', as indicated by the verse.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that there is a contradiction between Quran 16:78 ('you knew nothing') and the hadith 'every child is born upon the fitrah', often understood to mean that fitrah is Islam. The author provides two main arguments. First, if the apparent meanings conflict, we give precedence to the Quran, which explicitly says 'you knew nothing'. Second, the alleged contradiction only arises if we take the hadith according to the concept of opposition (mafhum al-mukhalafa). The author demonstrates that this concept does not apply here because: (a) a child before puberty is not legally considered Muslim or disbeliever except by affiliation to parents; (b) the concept of opposition would require restricting the newborn to only four religions, ignoring modern ideologies; and (c) there is another hadith that says the child's state is determined by his parents' religion, and yet another that says the child will be accounted for only when he can express himself. Therefore, the correct understanding is that fitrah is not innate knowledge but the natural state of ignorance, consistent with the verse.

Correction: The author clarifies that fitrah is not Islam but the state of ignorance, as the verse says 'you knew nothing'. Children are only judged by affiliation to parents until they reach maturity.

Checkpoint

B) Fitrah means the child is born Muslim by affiliation to parents.
C) Fitrah is the state of ignorance, as the verse says 'you knew nothing'.
D) Fitrah is the natural inclination to do good.

Key Takeaway
The correct understanding of fitrah is the state of ignorance, not innate knowledge of Islam, as the Quranic verse 'you knew nothing' clearly indicates.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author argues that your natural state at birth was complete ignorance — you knew nothing. How does this truth humble you when you consider the knowledge of Islam you now possess? Does it make you more grateful to Allah for guiding you, or does it fill you with urgency to learn more? What will you do today to acknowledge that your knowledge is a gift, not a birthright?
?

Are you willing to reexamine your own method of learning and prioritization? What cherished assumption about a hadith might you be holding onto that needs to be corrected by the Quran's clearer text?

Justification for Including Philosophical Arguments
The author justifies why later kalam scholars incorporated philosophical arguments into their works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why later kalam scholars included philosophical arguments in their works and how this was a defensive measure, not a deviation.

Core Idea
Later scholars used philosophy to defend Islam, not to adopt it.

This passage addresses a common critique: that later kalam scholars abandoned the pure method of the Salaf by using philosophical reasoning. The author of al-Jawharah explains the historical context: after 500 AH, innovators mixed false doubts with philosophical principles, forcing the later scholars to engage with those same principles in order to refute them effectively.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then read the English translation. Notice the cause-and-effect structure.
The passage is a single sentence with a clear historical trigger.
2
Identify the historical cause
Look for the event that prompted the later scholars to act. The passage mentions innovators appearing after 500 AH and mixing doubts with philosophical principles.
3
Understand the defensive purpose
The passage states that the scholars included philosophical arguments expressly to repulse doubts. Consider how this answers critics who accuse them of innovating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنه لما حدثت المبتدعة بعد الخمس مئة ، وكثر جدالهم مع علماء الإسلام، وأوردوا شُبَهاً على ما قرَّره الأوائل، وخلطوا تلك الشبه بكثير من القواعد الفلسفية، قصد المتأخرون دفع تلك الشبه، فاحتاجوا إلى إدراجها في كلامهم؛ ليتمكنوا من ردها، فما أدرجوها إلا لغرض مهم ؛ بحيث لا يبعد معه الوجوب، خلافاً لمن شنَّعَ عليهم في ذلك .
Because when the innovators arose after five hundred (AH), and their arguments increased against the scholars of Islam, and they brought forth doubts upon what the predecessors had established, and they mixed those doubts with many philosophical principles, the later scholars intended to repulse those doubts, so they needed to include them in their discourse; to be able to refute them. They only included them for an important purpose, such that obligation is not far from it, contrary to those who criticized them for that.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a historical justification for the inclusion of philosophical arguments by later kalam scholars (المتأخرون). It explains that because heretical innovators (المبتدعة) appeared after the Islamic calendar reached 500 AH, and they mixed their false doubts with philosophical principles, the later scholars had to incorporate those same principles into their works in order to refute them effectively. The author emphasizes that this was done only for the essential purpose of defending the truth, not as a deviation, and it answers those who criticized the use of philosophy in kalam.

Key Terms

المتأخرون
الشبه
القواعد الفلسفية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That kalam scholars included philosophy because they were influenced by it, not to refute it.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that they included these arguments only to repulse the doubts of innovators, for an important purpose, not due to influence.

Checkpoint

B) To repulse the doubts of innovators who used philosophical principles.
C) To make kalam more complicated.
D) Because it was the tradition of the predecessors.

Key Takeaway
The inclusion of philosophical arguments by later scholars was a necessary defensive measure against the innovators' misuse of philosophy.

The Three Types of Expression: Tawil, Ikhtisar, and Musawat
Commentary defining three types of expression and the need for conciseness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of expression in kalam works and why non-defective conciseness is necessary for teaching.

Core Idea
Conciseness is required to avoid wearying learners, but it must not be defective.

The commentator explains the poet's line about the need for conciseness. He defines three types of expression: expansion (tawil), conciseness (ikhtisar), and equality (musawat). This lesson explores these definitions and the rationale behind the emphasis on conciseness in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the three definitions and the reasoning behind the need for conciseness.
Notice the commentator's use of 'min' for explanation and the metaphor in 'aspirations became weary'.
2
Identify the three definitions
Extract the definitions of tawil, ikhtisar, and musawat from the passage. Understand how they differ in relation to customary expression.
Tawil uses more words, ikhtisar uses fewer, musawat uses the same.
3
Understand the condition
The passage states that conciseness is obligatory but only if it is non-defective. Reflect on why defective conciseness would be blameworthy.
Consider the purpose of teaching: clarity without obscurity.
4
Reflect on the concluding quote
The passage ends with a quote from Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini that all discussions of tawhid can be summed up in two words. Think about the power of concise expression.
This reinforces the value of ikhtisar when done properly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لَكِنْ مِنَ التَّطْوِيلِ كَلَّتِ الهِمَمْ فَصَارَ فِيهِ الاخْتِصَارُ مُلْتَزَمُ. قوله : (لَكِنْ ... إلى آخره: استدراك على قوله : (يحتاج للتبيين) لأنه يقتضي مزيد التطويل، فدفع ذلك بقوله : (لكن ... إلى آخره، فكأنه قال: هذا الفن وإن احتاج للتبيين إلا أنه لا ينبغي المبالغة معه في تطويل العبارة؛ لأنها تؤدي إلى الملل والسامة. وقوله : (مِنَ التَّطْوِيلِ) أي : من أجله وسببه، فـ (من) للتعليل، والمراد: التطويلُ الكامل (١) ، فـ (أل) فيه للكمال، فالمحذورُ إنما هو المبالغة فى التطويل، وأما أصلُ التطويل فلا يضرُّ ؛ والتطويلُ : أداء المقصود بلفظ زائد على المتعارف لأوساط الناس الذين ليس لهم فصاحة ولا بلاغةٌ، وأما الاختصارُ : فهو أداء المقصود بأقل من العبارة المتعارفة، والمساواة : أداء المقصود بلفظ مساو للمتعارف . قوله : (كَلْتِ الهِمَمْ) أي : تعبت أصحابها (٢) ، ففيه مجاز عقلي، و(الهمم): جمع همة؛ وهي لغة القوَّةُ والعزم، وعرفاً حالةٌ للنفس يتبعها غلبه انبعاث إلى نيل مقصود ما، ثم إن تعلَّقت بمعالي الأمور، فعليَّة، وإلا فدنيَّة، وإذا لم تتعلق بواحد منهما، فليست علية ولا دنية . قوله : (فَصَارَ فِيهِ الاِخْتِصَارُ مُلْتَزَمُ) هذه الفاء تفريعية على ما قبلها، والمعنى: فصار في هذا الفنّ تأليفاً وتدريساً الاختصارُ ملتزماً ؛ تقريباً على المتعلمين القاصرين، ولا يخفى أن الاختصار) اسم صار وملتزم) خبرها لكن وقف عليه بالسكون على لغة ربيعة (۳) ، والملتزم إنما هو الاختصارُ غير المخلّ، وإلا فهو مذموم، وقد كان الأستاذ أبو إسحاق الإسفرايني يقول : (جميع ما قاله المتكلمون في التوحيد قد جمعه أهل الحق في كلمتين :
But due to expansion, the aspirations became weary, so conciseness in it became obligatory. His statement: 'But...' etc. is a correction to his statement: 'needs clarification' because it implies excessive expansion. So he rebuts this with his statement: 'But...' etc. Thus it is as if he said: This discipline, although it needs clarification, yet one should not overdo expansion of expression, because it leads to boredom and weariness. And his statement: 'due to expansion' means because of it and its cause; so 'min' is for explanation, and the intended meaning is complete expansion, so 'al-' in it is for completeness. The harmful thing is only exaggeration in expansion, but the basis of expansion does not harm. And expansion is: conveying the intended meaning with extra wording beyond what is customary for the average people who have neither eloquence nor rhetoric. And conciseness is: conveying the intended meaning with less than the customary expression. And equality is: conveying the intended meaning with wording equal to the customary. His statement: 'aspirations became weary' means: their owners became tired, and it contains an intellectual metaphor. And 'al-himam' is the plural of himma, which linguistically means strength and determination, and technically is a state of the soul accompanied by a strong impulse to attain some goal. Then if it is attached to lofty matters, it is high, otherwise it is low, and if it is not attached to either, it is neither high nor low. His statement: 'so conciseness in it became obligatory' – this fa' is a result of what precedes it. The meaning: So in this discipline, in authorship and teaching, conciseness became obligatory, as an approximation for deficient learners. It is not hidden that 'al-ikhtisar' is the noun of 'sara' and 'multazam' is its predicate, but it is paused with sukun according to the dialect of Rabi'a. And what is obligatory is conciseness that is not defective, otherwise it is blameworthy. And the master Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini used to say: 'All that the mutakallimun have said about tawhid has been collected by the people of truth in two words:'
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of a line from the poem about the necessity of conciseness in kalam works. It defines three types of expression: expansion (tawil), conciseness (ikhtisar), and equality (musawat). The commentator explains that excessive expansion causes boredom, so conciseness is required, but it must be non-defective. He also analyzes the word 'himam' (aspirations) and the phrase 'conciseness became obligatory'. Finally, he quotes al-Isfarayini that all discussions of tawhid have been summed up in two words, though the quote is cut off. The lesson covers technical definitions and the principle that conciseness in teaching is necessary for students of limited ability.

Key Terms
التطويل
الاختصار
المساواة
الهمة
الملتزم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That conciseness (ikhtisar) means abbreviating to the point of obscurity.
Correction: The passage explicitly says that the obligatory conciseness is that which is not defective (غير المخل). Defective conciseness is blameworthy.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is 'ikhtisar' (اختصار)?
A) Conveying the intended meaning with more words than customary.
B) Conveying the intended meaning with fewer words than customary.
C) Conveying the intended meaning with the same number of words as customary.
D) Conveying the intended meaning without words.

Key Takeaway
Conciseness in kalam works is required to avoid wearying learners, but it must be non-defective to remain clear and effective.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study theology, do you ever choose a shorter explanation because you feel bored, even though you know it is incomplete? How does that choice reflect your himma?
?
The commentator warns that defective conciseness is blameworthy. Think of a time when you settled for a shallow understanding of an ‘aqeedah issue to save time. What did you lose, and what will you do differently now?
?
Your himma (aspiration) attaches to goals—lofty or low. If your desire for quick knowledge is driven by laziness, not necessity, then your hisma is low. Will you commit to enduring careful study, even if it expands the time, so that your understanding is not defective?
